1572<p id="_1069" tabindex="-1">

1573This section provides an overview of critical elements in the cancer <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460214&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460214&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1574risk assessment</a>

1575 process. </p>

1576<p id="_327" tabindex="-1">

1577A number of professional guidelines on the elements of cancer genetics risk assessment and <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1578counseling</a>

1579 are available.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

15801</a>

1581-<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

15825</a>

1583] Except where noted, the discussion below is based on these guidelines.</p>

1584<p id="_328" tabindex="-1">

1585The cancer risk assessment and genetic counseling process consists of one or more consultative sessions and generally includes the following:</p>

1586<div class="pdq-content-list">

1587<ul id="_329">

1588<li>

1589A detailed, multifaceted assessment including medical, psychosocial, and family history.</li>

1590<li>

1591A determination of the risk of cancer and/or indication for genetic testing based on evidence of an <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339343&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339343&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1592inherited cancer syndrome</a>

1593.</li>

1594<li>

1595Education and counseling about <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460148&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460148&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1596familial</a>

1597/hereditary cancer risks.</li>

1598<li>

1599If appropriate, review of genetic testing options as well as potential limitations, risks, and benefits of testing.</li>

1600<li>

1601Establishment of a cancer risk management plan.</li>

1602<li>

1603Discussion of follow-up plans, provision of referrals, educational materials, etc.</li>

1604</ul>

1605</div>

1606<section id="_1070">

1607<h3 id="_1070_toc">

1608Assessment</h3>

1609<p id="_333" tabindex="-1">

1610At the outset of the initial counseling session, eliciting and addressing the <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460140&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460140&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1611consultand's</a>

1612 perceptions and concerns about cancer and his or her expectations of the risk assessment process helps to engage the consultand in the session. This also helps inform the provider about practical or psychosocial issues and guides the focus of counseling and strategies for risk assessment. </p>

1613<section id="_336">

1614<h4 id="_336_toc">

1615Psychosocial assessment</h4>

1616<p id="_337" tabindex="-1">

1617The counseling process that takes place as part of a cancer risk assessment can identify factors that contribute to the consultand's perception of cancer risk and motivations to seek cancer risk assessment and genetic testing. It can also identify potential psychological issues that may need to be addressed during or after the session, particularly after genetic testing. Information collected before and/or during the session may include the following:</p>

1618<div class="pdq-content-list">

1619<ul id="_338">

1620<li>

1621Motivations for seeking cancer risk assessment.</li>

1622<li>

1623Beliefs about the causes of cancer.</li>

1624<li>

1625Experiences with cancer and feelings, perceptions, concerns, or fears related to those experiences.</li>

1626<li>

1627The influence of cancer experiences and perceptions on health behaviors and cancer <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000046171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1628screening</a>

1629 practices.</li>

1630<li>

1631Cultural, religious, and socioeconomic background.</li>

1632<li>

1633General psychological history, such as depression or anxiety, and medication use.</li>

1634<li>

1635Coping mechanisms.</li>

1636<li>

1637Support systems.</li>

1638</ul>

1639</div>

1640<p id="_339" tabindex="-1">

1641Either alone or in consultation with a mental health provider, health care providers offering cancer risk counseling attempt to assess whether there are factors suggesting risk of adverse psychological outcomes after disclosure of risk and/or genetic status. </p>

1642</section>

1643<section id="_1072">

1644<h4 id="_1072_toc">

1645Risk perception</h4>

1646<p id="_1075" tabindex="-1">

1647Perceived risk can play an important role in an individual&#8217;s decision to

1648participate in counseling,[<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

16496</a>

1650] despite the fact that perceived risk often

1651varies substantially from statistical risk estimates.[<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

16527</a>

1653-<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

16549</a>

1655]</p>

1656</section>

1657</section>

1658<section id="_182">

1659<h3 id="_182_toc">

1660Clinical Evaluation</h3>

1661<section id="_184">

1662<h4 id="_184_toc">

1663Personal health history</h4>

1664<p id="_185" tabindex="-1">

1665Consideration of the consultand's personal health history is essential in cancer risk assessment, regardless of whether the individual has a personal history of cancer. Important information to obtain about the consultand's health history includes the following:[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

16661</a>

1667,<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

16683</a>

1669] </p>

1670<div class="pdq-content-list">

1671<ul id="_1076">

1672<li>

1673Current age.</li>

1674<li>

1675Race, ancestry, and ethnicity.</li>

1676<li>

1677History of benign or precancerous tumors or polyps, surgeries, biopsies, major illnesses, medications, and reproductive history (for women, this includes age at menarche, parity, age at first live birth, age at menopause, and history of exogenous hormone use).</li>

1678<li>

1679Screening practices and date of last screening exams, including imaging and/or physical examinations.</li>

1680<li>

1681Environmental exposures.</li>

1682<li>

1683Past and current alcohol intake and tobacco use.</li>

1684<li>

1685Diet, exercise, and complementary and alternative medicine practices may also be assessed.</li>

1686</ul>

1687</div>

1688<p id="_1077" tabindex="-1">

1689For consultands with a history of cancer, additional information collected includes the following:</p>

1690<div class="pdq-content-list">

1691<ul id="_1078">

1692<li>

1693Site/type of primary malignancy and any metastasis or recurrence.</li>

1694<li>

1695Age at diagnosis.</li>

1696<li>

1697Pathology findings/staging.</li>

1698<li>

1699Prior <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1700germline</a>

1701 genetic testing results.</li>

1702<li>

1703Prior tumor testing results (including genomic profiling). For more information on the implications of tumor testing, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/overview-pdq#_2594">

1704Clinical Sequencing</a>

1705 section in Cancer Genetics Overview.</li>

1706<li>

1707Treatment (e.g., surgery, chemotherapy, radiation therapy, targeted therapy), including whether genetic risk assessment may affect treatment.</li>

1708<li>

1709Bilaterality of disease, if applicable.</li>

1710<li>

1711Current <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=496506&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000496506&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1712surveillance</a>

1713 plan.</li>

1714<li>

1715Carcinogenic exposures (e.g., alcohol and tobacco use, sun exposure, radiation exposure, asbestos exposure) or other known cancer site-specific risk factors.</li>

1716<li>

1717How the cancer was detected (e.g., self-exam, screening test, presenting symptoms) may also be assessed.</li>

1718</ul>

1719</div>

1720</section>

1721<section id="_186">

1722<h4 id="_186_toc">

1723Physical examination</h4>

1724<p id="_187" tabindex="-1">

1725In some cases, a physical exam is conducted by a qualified medical professional to determine whether the individual has physical findings suggestive of a hereditary cancer predisposition syndrome or to rule out evidence of an existing malignancy. For example, a medical professional may look for the sebaceous adenomas seen in Muir-Torre syndrome, measure the head circumference or perform a skin exam to rule out benign cutaneous features associated with Cowden syndrome, or perform a clinical breast and axillary lymph node exam on a woman undergoing a breast cancer risk assessment.</p>

1726</section>

1727<section id="_188">

1728<h4 id="_188_toc">

1729Family history</h4>

1730<section id="_162">

1731<h5 id="_162_toc">

1732Documenting the family history</h5>

1733<p id="_163" tabindex="-1">

1734The family history is an essential tool for cancer risk assessment. The family history can be obtained via interview or written self-report; both were found to result in equivalent information.[<a href="#cit/section_5.10">

173510</a>

1736] Studies suggest that paper-based family history questionnaires completed before the appointment provide accurate family history information [<a href="#cit/section_5.11">

173711</a>

1738] and that the use of these questionnaires is an acceptable and understandable family history collection method.[<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

173912</a>

1740] Both multimedia-based (e.g., Internet) and print-based (e.g., family history questionnaires) tools are currently available to gather information about family history. However, on average, print-based tools have been found to be written at lower reading grade levels than multimedia-based tools.[<a href="#cit/section_5.13">

174113</a>

1742] It has been reported that questionnaire-based assessments may lead to some underreporting of family history; therefore, a follow-up interview to confirm the reported information and to capture all relevant family history information may be required.[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

174314</a>

1744] Collecting family history from multiple relatives in a single family has been shown to increase the number of family members reported to have cancer, compared with family history information provided by a single family member.[<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

174515</a>

1746]</p>

1747<p id="_1362" tabindex="-1">

1748 Details of the family health history are best summarized in the form of a family tree, or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1749pedigree</a>

1750. The pedigree, a standardized graphic representation of family relationships, facilitates identification of patterns of disease transmission, recognition of the clinical characteristics associated with specific hereditary cancer syndromes, and determination of the best strategies and tools for risk assessment.[<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

175116</a>

1752,<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

175317</a>

1754] </p>

1755<p id="_165" tabindex="-1">

1756Standards of pedigree nomenclature have been established.[<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

175716</a>

1758,<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

175917</a>

1760] Notably, several different pedigree symbols have been suggested to represent transgender or gender nonconforming individuals.[<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

176117</a>

1762,<a href="#cit/section_5.18">

176318</a>

1764] The genetic counseling community has not agreed on best approaches for representing these individuals on a pedigree.[<a href="#cit/section_5.19">

176519</a>

1766] For more information on common pedigree symbols, see <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1237">

1767Figure 1</a>

1768.</p>

1769<figure id="figure_1237" class="image-center">

1770<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/613538.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1771Enlarge</a>

1772<img id="_1237" alt="Standard pedigree nomenclature; diagram shows common symbols used to draw a pedigree." title="Standard pedigree nomenclature; diagram shows common symbols used to draw a pedigree." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/613538-750.jpg">

1773<figcaption class="caption-container">

1774Figure 1. Standard pedigree nomenclature. Common symbols are used to draw a pedigree (family tree). A pedigree shows relationships between family members and patterns of inheritance for certain traits and diseases.</figcaption>

1775</figure>

1776<p id="_166" tabindex="-1">

1777Documentation of a comprehensive family cancer history typically includes the following:</p>

1778<div class="pdq-content-list">

1779<ul id="_167">

1780<li>

1781A three-generation pedigree consisting of a minimum of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460150&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460150&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1782first-</a>

1783 and <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=485395&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000485395&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1784second-degree relatives</a>

1785 on both the maternal and paternal sides of the family. Information on multiple generations helps to demonstrate inheritance patterns. Hereditary cancer can be inherited from either the maternal or paternal side of the family and is often an adult-onset disease.[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

178620</a>

1787]</li>

1788<li>

1789Race, ancestry, and ethnicity of all grandparents. This may influence decisions about genetic testing because specific <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1790pathogenic variants</a>

1791 in some <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000045693&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1792genes</a>

1793 are known to occur with increased frequency in some populations (<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783962&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783962&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1794founder effect</a>

1795).[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

179620</a>

1797]</li>

1798<li>

1799Information about seemingly unrelated conditions, such as birth defects, atypical skin bumps, or other nonmalignant conditions of children and adults that may aid in the diagnosis of a cancer susceptibility syndrome.</li>

1800<li>

1801Notation of adoption, nonpaternity (the biologic father should be included in the pedigree), <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460138&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460138&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1802consanguinity</a>

1803, and use of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339335&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339335&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1804assisted reproductive technology</a>

1805 (e.g., donor egg or sperm).</li>

1806</ul>

1807</div>

1808<p id="_168" tabindex="-1">

1809A three-generation family history includes the following:</p>

1810<div class="pdq-content-list">

1811<ul id="_169">

1812<li>

1813First-degree relatives (e.g., children, brothers and sisters, and parents).</li>

1814<li>

1815Second-degree relatives (e.g., grandparents, aunts and uncles, nieces and nephews, grandchildren, and half-siblings).</li>

1816<li>

1817Third-degree relatives (e.g., first cousins, great aunts, and great uncles).</li>

1818<li>

1819Additional distant relatives are included if information is available, especially when there are known cancer histories among them.</li>

1820</ul>

1821</div>

1822<p id="_170" tabindex="-1">

1823For any relative with cancer, collect the following information:[<a href="#cit/section_5.21">

182421</a>

1825]</p>

1826<div class="pdq-content-list">

1827<ul id="_171">

1828<li>

1829Primary site of each cancer. Obtaining medical documentation of key cancers (e.g., pathology reports, clinical documents, and death certificates) is especially relevant to risk assessment and/or management recommendations.</li>

1830<li>

1831Age at diagnosis for each primary cancer.</li>

1832<li>

1833Where the relative was diagnosed and/or treated.</li>

1834<li>

1835History of surgery or treatments that may have reduced the risk of cancer. For example, bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy in a premenopausal woman significantly reduces the risk of ovarian and breast cancers. This may mask underlying hereditary predisposition to these cancers. </li>

1836<li>

1837Current age (if living).</li>

1838<li>

1839Age at death and cause of death (if deceased).</li>

1840<li>

1841Carcinogenic exposures (e.g., alcohol and tobacco use, sun exposure, radiation exposure, asbestos exposure) or other known cancer site-specific risk factors.</li>

1842<li>

1843Prior germline genetic testing results.</li>

1844<li>

1845Prior tumor testing results (including genomic profiling).</li>

1846<li>

1847Other significant health problems.</li>

1848<li>

1849For more information, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_340">

1850Accuracy of the family history</a>

1851 section.</li>

1852</ul>

1853</div>

1854<p id="_172" tabindex="-1">

1855For relatives not <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460124&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460124&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

1856affected</a>

1857 with cancer, collect the following information:</p>

1858<div class="pdq-content-list">

1859<ul id="_173">

1860<li>

1861Current age or age at death.</li>

1862<li>

1863Cause of death (if deceased).</li>

1864<li>

1865History of any surgeries or treatments that may have reduced the risk of cancer.</li>

1866<li>

1867Cancer screening practices.</li>

1868<li>

1869Any nonmalignant features associated with the syndrome in question.</li>

1870<li>

1871Carcinogenic exposures (e.g., alcohol and tobacco use, sun exposure, radiation exposure, asbestos exposure) or other known cancer site-specific risk factors.</li>

1872<li>

1873Prior germline genetic testing results.</li>

1874<li>

1875Prior tumor testing results (including genomic profiling).</li>

1876<li>

1877Other significant health problems.</li>

1878</ul>

1879</div>

1880</section>

1881<section id="_340">

1882<h5 id="_340_toc">

1883Accuracy of the family history</h5>

1884<p id="_341" tabindex="-1">

1885The accuracy of the family history has a direct bearing on determining the differential diagnoses, selecting appropriate testing, interpreting results of the genetic tests, refining individual cancer risk estimates, and outlining screening and risk reduction recommendations. In a telephone survey of 1,019 individuals, only 6% did not know whether a first-degree relative had cancer; this increased to 8.5% for second-degree relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_5.22">

188622</a>

1887] However, people often have incomplete or inaccurate information about the cancer history in their family.[<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

188817</a>

1889,<a href="#cit/section_5.21">

189021</a>

1891,<a href="#cit/section_5.23">

189223</a>

1893-<a href="#cit/section_5.29">

189429</a>

1895] </p>

1896<p id="_1944" tabindex="-1">

1897Patient education has been shown to improve the completeness of family history collection and may lead to more-accurate risk stratification, referrals for genetic counseling, and changes to management recommendations.[<a href="#cit/section_5.30">

189830</a>

1899] Confirming the primary site of cancers in the family that will affect the calculation of hereditary predisposition probabilities and/or estimation of empiric cancer risks may be important, especially if decisions about treatments such as risk-reducing surgery will be based on this family history.[<a href="#cit/section_5.25">

190025</a>

1901,<a href="#cit/section_5.31">

190231</a>

1903] </p>

1904<p id="_1378" tabindex="-1">

1905Accuracy varies by cancer site and degree of relatedness.[<a href="#cit/section_5.27">

190627</a>

1907,<a href="#cit/section_5.32">

190832</a>

1909,<a href="#cit/section_5.33">

191033</a>

1911] Reporting of cancer family histories may be most accurate for breast cancer [<a href="#cit/section_5.27">

191227</a>

1913,<a href="#cit/section_5.33">

191433</a>

1915] and less accurate for gynecologic malignancies [<a href="#cit/section_5.27">

191627</a>

1917,<a href="#cit/section_5.33">

191833</a>

1919] and colon cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.27">

192027</a>

1921] Self-reported family histories may contain errors and, in rare instances, could be fictitious.[<a href="#cit/section_5.25">

192225</a>

1923,<a href="#cit/section_5.31">

192431</a>

1925,<a href="#cit/section_5.33">

192633</a>

1927] The most reliable documentation of cancer histology is the pathology report. Verification of cancers can also be made through other medical records, tumor registries, or death certificates. </p>

1928</section>

1929</section>

1930</section>

1931<section id="_1079">

1932<h3 id="_1079_toc">

1933Determining Cancer Risk</h3>

1934<section id="_174">

1935<h4 id="_174_toc">

1936Analysis of the family history</h4>

1937<p id="_175" tabindex="-1">

1938Because a family history of cancer is one of the important predictors of cancer

1939risk, analysis of the pedigree constitutes an important aspect of risk

1940assessment. This analysis might be thought of as a series of the following questions:

1941</p>

1942<div class="pdq-content-list">

1943<ul id="_1192">

1944<li>

1945<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_44">

1946What evidence suggests that a cancer susceptibility syndrome is present in

1947this family?</a>

1948</li>

1949<li>

1950<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1530">

1951If a syndrome is suspected, what are the differential diagnoses?</a>

1952</li>

1953<li>

1954<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_64">

1955What factors can make the family history difficult to interpret?</a>

1956</li>

1957<li>

1958<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1538">

1959What is the most likely mode of inheritance for a hereditary cancer syndrome?</a>

1960</li>

1961<li>

1962<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_66">

1963What is a family member's risk of developing cancer if an inherited susceptibility

1964exists?</a>

1965</li>

1966<li>

1967<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_67">

1968If a recognizable hereditary cancer syndrome is not found, what is an individual's cancer risk based on other

1969epidemiological risk factors?</a>

1970</li>

1971</ul>

1972</div>

1973<div class="pdq-content-list">

1974<ol id="_178">

1975<li>

1976<strong>

1977What evidence suggests that a cancer susceptibility syndrome is present in

1978this family?</strong>

1979<p id="_44" tabindex="-1">

1980Hereditary cancer syndromes are found by analyzing both pedigrees and physical

1981findings. The index of suspicion is raised by the following:[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

198220</a>

1983]</p>

1984<div class="pdq-content-list">

1985<ul id="_45">

1986<li>

1987Multiple cancers in close relatives, particularly in multiple generations.</li>

1988<li>

1989Early age of cancer onset (younger than age 40 to 50 y for adult-onset cancers).

1990</li>

1991<li>

1992Multiple primary cancers in a single individual.

1993</li>

1994<li>

1995Bilateral cancers.</li>

1996<li>

1997Recognition of the known association between etiologically related cancers

1998in the family (e.g., breast and ovarian cancers; colon and endometrial cancers).

1999</li>

2000<li>

2001Presence of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460137&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460137&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2002congenital</a>

2003 anomalies or precursor lesions that are known to be

2004associated with increased cancer risk (e.g., presence of atypical nevi and

2005risk of malignant melanoma).

2006</li>

2007<li>

2008Recognizable mendelian inheritance pattern.

2009</li>

2010<li>

2011Specific tumor types or pathologies associated with <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=781852&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000781852&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2012germline pathogenic variants</a>

2013 in cancer <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460209&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460209&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2014susceptibility genes</a>

2015, regardless of family history (e.g., ovarian cancer, medullary thyroid cancer, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=639916&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000639916&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2016triple-negative breast cancer</a>

2017, sex cord tumors in ovarian cancer). For more information on these tumor types, see <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq">

2018Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers</a>

2019 and <a href="/types/thyroid/hp/medullary-thyroid-genetics-pdq">

2020Genetics of Endocrine and Neuroendocrine Neoplasias</a>

2021.</li>

2022<li>

2023Abnormal results from colon or endometrial tumor testing with <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285933&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000285933&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2024microsatellite instability</a>

2025 or immunohistochemistry, suggestive of Lynch syndrome. For more information, see the <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_1102">

2026Genetics of Lynch syndrome</a>

2027 section in Genetics of Colorectal Cancer.</li>

2028<li>

2029Somatic <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46063&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000046063&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2030mutations</a>

2031 identified from tumor genomic profiling that may be present in the germline.</li>

2032</ul>

2033</div>

2034<p id="_1555" tabindex="-1">

2035Clinical characteristics associated with different cancer genetic syndromes are summarized in the following <a href="https://www.nature.com/articles/gim2014147#recommendations" title="https://www.nature.com/articles/gim2014147#recommendations">

2036comprehensive set of personal and family history criteria</a>

2037 published by the American College of Medical Genetics and Genomics (ACMG) and the National Society of Genetic Counselors (NSGC).[<a href="#cit/section_5.34">

203834</a>

2039] These practice guidelines address tumor types, other potential features, and related criteria that would prompt a genetics referral. ACMG and NSGC state that the guidelines are intended to maximize appropriate referral of at-risk individuals for cancer genetics consultations, but they are not meant to provide genetic testing or treatment recommendations. However, these organizations also note the importance of consulting other guidelines that are more frequently updated, like the National Comprehensive Cancer Network.[<a href="#cit/section_5.35">

204035</a>

2041]</p>

2042</li>

2043<li>

2044<strong>

2045If a syndrome is suspected, what are the differential diagnoses?</strong>

2046<p id="_1530" tabindex="-1">

2047The most common indications for genetic counseling/testing are suspected hereditary breast cancer or hereditary colon cancer syndromes.</p>

2048<p id="_1531" tabindex="-1">

2049For hereditary breast cancer, genetic counseling and testing criteria are broad.[<a href="#cit/section_5.18">

205018</a>

2051,<a href="#cit/section_5.34">

205234</a>

2053] <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=775581&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000775581&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2054Multigene panel testing</a>

2055 has revealed that pathogenic variants in several other high- and moderate-<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339344&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339344&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2056penetrance</a>

2057 genes other than <em class="gene-name">

2058BRCA1</em>

2059 and <em class="gene-name">

2060BRCA2</em>

2061 contribute to this <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460203&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460203&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2062phenotype</a>

2063, such as <em class="gene-name">

2064PALB2</em>

2065, <em class="gene-name">

2066CHEK2</em>

2067, and <em class="gene-name">

2068ATM</em>

2069. </p>

2070<p id="_1532" tabindex="-1">

2071Differential diagnoses for hereditary colon cancer syndromes are based on several factors, including the following: number of colorectal polyps, type of colorectal polyps, histopathology of gastrointestinal malignancies, and histopathology of other malignancies.[<a href="#cit/section_5.36">

207236</a>

2073,<a href="#cit/section_5.37">

207437</a>

2075] However, in the absence of polyposis and rare pathologies, Lynch syndrome is often on the differential diagnosis list. Furthermore, Lynch syndrome may be on the differential diagnosis list when there are cases of breast and/or ovarian cancer in the family that are not consistent with hereditary breast and ovarian cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.38">

207638</a>

2077,<a href="#cit/section_5.39">

207839</a>

2079] For more information, see the <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_2986">

2080Lynch syndrome</a>

2081 section in Genetics of Colorectal Cancer.</p>

2082<p id="_1533" tabindex="-1">

2083Diagnostic and testing criteria exist for several rare syndromes such as <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_3755">

2084Li-Fraumeni syndrome</a>

2085,[<a href="#cit/section_5.40">

208640</a>

2087] <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_2559">

2088Cowden syndrome</a>

2089,[<a href="#cit/section_5.41">

209041</a>

2091,<a href="#cit/section_5.42">

209242</a>

2093] <a href="/types/thyroid/hp/medullary-thyroid-genetics-pdq#_760">

2094multiple endocrine neoplasias</a>

2095,[<a href="#cit/section_5.43">

209643</a>

2097] and <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_3205">

2098familial adenomatous polyposis</a>

2099.[<a href="#cit/section_5.36">

210036</a>

2101] In some cases, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454769&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000454769&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2102pathognomonic</a>

2103 features are indicators for rare cancer syndromes.[<a href="#cit/section_5.41">

210441</a>

2105,<a href="#cit/section_5.42">

210642</a>

2107]</p>

2108<p id="_1534" tabindex="-1">

2109Based on these considerations, genetic testing options include targeted testing for pathogenic variants in one or several genes or testing with a large gene panel.</p>

2110</li>

2111<li>

2112<strong>

2113What factors can make the family history difficult to interpret?</strong>

2114<p id="_64" tabindex="-1">

2115Other factors may complicate recognition of

2116basic inheritance patterns or represent different types of disease

2117etiology.[<a href="#cit/section_5.44">

211844</a>

2119-<a href="#cit/section_5.46">

212046</a>

2121] </p>

2122<p id="_1535" tabindex="-1">

2123Common examples of factors that complicate family history structure include the following:

2124</p>

2125<div class="pdq-content-list">

2126<ul id="_65">

2127<li>

2128Small family size.</li>

2129<li>

2130Incomplete information due to a lack of family history knowledge (e.g., due to adoption or lack of information about cancers in relatives).</li>

2131<li>

2132Gender imbalance (e.g., few women in a family suspected of hereditary breast cancer).</li>

2133<li>

2134Deaths at particularly early ages.</li>

2135<li>

2136Removal of the at-risk organ, either for risk reduction or as a result of a medical condition (e.g., hysterectomy due to history of uterine fibroids or endometriosis may hamper the identification of Lynch syndrome).</li>

2137<li>

2138Misattributed parentage.

2139</li>

2140<li>

2141Consanguinity.</li>

2142</ul>

2143</div>

2144<p id="_1536" tabindex="-1">

2145Genetic factors that may affect family history interpretation include:</p>

2146<div class="pdq-content-list">

2147<ul id="_1537">

2148<li>

2149<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339332&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339332&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2150Late or variable onset</a>

2151 of disease.</li>

2152<li>

2153<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339330&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339330&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2154Nonpenetrance</a>

2155.

2156</li>

2157<li>

2158<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339331&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339331&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2159Variable expression</a>

2160.

2161</li>

2162<li>

2163<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339333&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339333&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2164Genetic heterogeneity</a>

2165.

2166</li>

2167<li>

2168<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783882&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783882&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2169De novo pathogenic variant</a>

2170.

2171</li>

2172<li>

2173<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460197&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460197&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2174Mosaicism</a>

2175 (somatic or germline).

2176 </li>

2177</ul>

2178</div>

2179</li>

2180<li>

2181<strong>

2182What is the most likely mode of inheritance for a hereditary cancer syndrome?</strong>

2183<p id="_1538" tabindex="-1">

2184The <em>

2185<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460196&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460196&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2186mode of inheritance</a>

2187</em>

2188 refers to the way that genetic traits are transmitted in a family. </p>

2189<p id="_1539" tabindex="-1">

2190Most modes of inheritance are established when the patient is given a clinical diagnosis and when a compatible, but not necessarily conclusive, inheritance pattern is seen on a pedigree.[<a href="#cit/section_5.47">

219147</a>

2192] Most recognized hereditary cancer syndromes are inherited in an<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=793860&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000793860&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2193 autosomal dominant</a>

2194 or an <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339339&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339339&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2195autosomal recessive</a>

2196 fashion. Clues to recognizing these patterns within a pedigree are described below.</p>

2197<div class="pdq-content-list">

2198<p class="pdq-list-title">

2199Autosomal dominant

2200</p>

2201<ul id="_49">

2202<li>

2203<em>

2204Autosomal dominant inheritance</em>

2205 refers to disorders that are expressed in heterozygotes (i.e., the affected person has one copy of a pathogenic variant and one

2206normal copy of the gene). All of the major hereditary breast/gynecological and colorectal cancer syndromes are inherited in an autosomal dominant fashion; these syndromes include the following: <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/brca-genes-hp-pdq">

2207BRCA1- and BRCA2-associated hereditary breast and gynecologic cancers</a>

2208, <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_3755">

2209Li-Fraumeni syndrome</a>

2210, <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_2559">

2211Cowden syndrome</a>

2212, <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_2986">

2213Lynch syndrome</a>

2214. Autosomal dominant inheritance is

2215characterized by the following factors:

2216<div class="pdq-content-list">

2217<ul id="_50" class="list-dash">

2218<li>

2219Vertical occurrence (i.e., syndrome-associated cancers are seen in successive generations).

2220</li>

2221<li>

2222Syndrome-associated cancers are seen only on one side of the family (i.e., unipaternal or unimaternal).

2223</li>

2224<li>

2225Males and females may inherit and transmit the disorder to offspring.

2226</li>

2227<li>

2228Male-to-male transmission may be seen.

2229</li>

2230<li>

2231Offspring have a 50% chance of inheriting a pathogenic variant and a 50% chance of

2232inheriting a normal copy of the gene.

2233</li>

2234<li>

2235The condition may appear to skip a generation due to several factors: <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=792352&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000792352&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2236incomplete penetrance</a>

2237,

2238 early death, delayed age of disease onset, or a small number of affected males/females when the at-risk organ is gender-specific (e.g., prostate and ovary).</li>

2239<li>

2240It is possible for an individual to have a de novo (new) pathogenic variant. This person would be the first affected member of his or her family, but he/she could transmit this pathogenic variant to his/her offspring in an autosomal dominant fashion.</li>

2241<li>

2242It is possible for an individual to have pathogenic variants in more than one gene associated with known autosomal dominant inherited cancer predisposition syndromes. In families with phenotypes suggestive of more than one type of hereditary cancer syndrome, identifying multiple pathogenic variants helps better explain complex personal and/or family histories of cancer. This can help determine the appropriate testing strategy for family members.[<a href="#cit/section_5.48">

224348</a>

2244]</li>

2245</ul>

2246</div>

2247</li>

2248</ul>

2249</div>

2250<div class="pdq-content-list">

2251<p class="pdq-list-title">

2252Autosomal recessive</p>

2253<ul id="_51">

2254<li>

2255In <em>

2256autosomal recessive inheritance</em>

2257, an

2258affected person must be <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339342&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339342&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2259homozygous</a>

2260 (i.e., an individual has two pathogenic variants and inherited one

2261pathogenic variant from each parent). Well-defined cancer susceptibility syndromes with autosomal recessive inheritance patterns include the following: <a href="/types/skin/hp/skin-genetics-pdq#_155">

2262Bloom syndrome</a>

2263, <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_1311">

2264ataxia telangiectasia</a>

2265, <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_433">

2266<em class="gene-name">

2267MUTYH</em>

2268-associated polyposis</a>

2269, and <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_2779">

2270Fanconi anemia</a>

2271. Autosomal recessive inheritance is characterized by the following:

2272<div class="pdq-content-list">

2273<ul id="_52" class="list-dash">

2274<li>

2275Horizontal occurrence (i.e., syndrome-associated cancers are only seen in one generation; affected siblings are seen in the absence of affected parents). This is generally not seen in successive generations.

2276</li>

2277<li>

2278Pathogenic variants are inherited from both sides of the family (i.e., biparental

2279inheritance).

2280</li>

2281<li>

2282Parents are <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339341&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339341&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2283heterozygous</a>

2284 <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460132&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460132&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2285carriers</a>

2286; each parent carries one pathogenic variant and one functional copy of the gene.</li>

2287<li>

2288Parents usually do not express the features associated with the pathogenic variant that they carry; in some

2289cases, parents may show a mild version of some features.

2290</li>

2291<li>

2292When two parents are heterozygous, there is a 25% risk for future offspring to be affected (homozygous).</li>

2293</ul>

2294</div>

2295</li>

2296</ul>

2297</div>

2298<div class="pdq-content-list">

2299<p class="pdq-list-title">

2300Complex inheritance</p>

2301<ul id="_56">

2302<li>

2303Most cancers, and most familial cancers, appear to have a complex etiology. Within clinical settings, negative or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=556485&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000556485&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2304uninformative</a>

2305 genetic testing results are common. Multiple factors may contribute to the development of the observed cancer(s); these factors may be difficult to pinpoint.</li>

2306<li>

2307<em>

2308Complex</em>

2309 or <em>

2310multifactorial disease inheritance</em>

2311 is used to describe conditions caused by genetic and environmental factors. In contrast to mendelian diseases, where carrying one pathogenic variant is associated with a high likelihood for developing the disease, complex/multifactorial diseases are caused by the interaction of genes and environmental factors. Therefore, a single genetic <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460162&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460162&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2312locus</a>

2313 is not responsible for the condition. In most cases, the combined effects of genetic, lifestyle, and environmental factors determine a person&#8217;s likelihood of being affected with a condition, such as cancer.

2314<p id="_58" tabindex="-1">

2315Clustering of cancer among relatives is common. However, determining the underlying

2316causes of this is difficult when there is not a clear pattern of cancer in the family. Individuals with common malignancies, like lung cancer, can have many relatives with cancer. These familial aggregations are thought to be caused by a combination of the following: exposures to known carcinogens (such as tobacco smoke), pathogenic variants in high-penetrance cancer risk genes, and alterations in low-penetrance genes that affect the metabolism of carcinogens.[<a href="#cit/section_5.49">

231749</a>

2318]</p>

2319<p id="_1239" tabindex="-1">

2320The general practitioner is likely to encounter some families with strong <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460153&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460153&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2321genetic predispositions</a>

2322 to cancer. Recognizing an individual's cancer susceptibility may have dramatic consequences for his/her health and management.

2323Although some high-risk pathogenic variants in major cancer susceptibility genes are consistent with recognizable

2324mendelian inheritance patterns, these syndromes are rare. </p>

2325</li>

2326</ul>

2327</div>

2328</li>

2329<li>

2330<strong>

2331What is a family member's risk of developing cancer if an inherited susceptibility

2332exists?</strong>

2333<p id="_66" tabindex="-1">

2334These probabilities vary by syndrome, family,

2335gene, and pathogenic variant. Different pathogenic variants in the same gene can

2336confer varying cancer risks, or the same pathogenic variant can be associated with

2337different clinical manifestations in separate families. Many of these scenarios can be attributed to penetrance and expressivity. For more information, see the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_2730">

2338Penetrance of Inherited Susceptibility to Hereditary Breast and/or Gynecologic Cancers</a>

2339 section in Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers.</p>

2340</li>

2341<li>

2342<strong>

2343If a recognizable hereditary cancer syndrome is not found, what is an individual's cancer risk based on other

2344epidemiological risk factors?</strong>

2345<p id="_67" tabindex="-1">

2346A positive family history may provide information about cancer risk, even when a hereditary cancer syndrome is not found. For example, the risk

2347of having a single relative affected with breast or colorectal

2348cancer can be estimated from epidemiologic data and family

2349studies. For more information, see <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq">

2350Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers</a>

2351 and <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq">

2352Genetics of

2353Colorectal Cancer</a>

2354.</p>

2355</li>

2356</ol>

2357</div>

2358</section>

2359<section id="_190">

2360<h4 id="_190_toc">

2361Methods of quantifying cancer risk</h4>

2362<p id="_191" tabindex="-1">

2363The overarching goal of cancer risk assessment is to individualize cancer risk management recommendations based on personalized risk. Methods to calculate risk utilize health history information and risk factor and family history data often in combination with emerging biologic and genetic/genomic evidence to establish predictions.[<a href="#cit/section_5.50">

236450</a>

2365] Multiple methodologies are used to calculate risk, including statistical models, prevalence data from specific populations, penetrance data when a documented pathogenic variant has been identified in a family, mendelian inheritance, and Bayesian analysis. All models have distinct capabilities, weaknesses, and limitations based on the methodology, sample size, and/or population used to create the model. Methods to individually quantify risk encompass two primary areas: the probability of harboring a pathogenic variant in a cancer susceptibility gene and the risk of developing a specific form of cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.50">

236650</a>

2367]</p>

2368<section id="_192">

2369<h5 id="_192_toc">

2370Risk of harboring a pathogenic variant in a cancer susceptibility gene</h5>

2371<p id="_193" tabindex="-1">

2372The decision to offer genetic testing for cancer susceptibility is complex and can be aided in part by objectively assessing an individual's and/or family's probability of harboring a pathogenic variant.[<a href="#cit/section_5.51">

237351</a>

2374] Predicting the probability of harboring a pathogenic variant in a cancer susceptibility gene can be done using several strategies, including empiric data, statistical models, population prevalence data, Mendel&#8217;s laws, Bayesian analysis, and specific health information, such as tumor-specific features.[<a href="#cit/section_5.51">

237551</a>

2376,<a href="#cit/section_5.52">

237752</a>

2378] All of these methods are gene specific or cancer-syndrome specific and are employed only after a thorough assessment has been completed and genetic differential diagnoses have been established.</p>

2379<p id="_292" tabindex="-1">

2380If a gene or hereditary cancer syndrome is suspected, syndrome-specific models can be used to determine if genetic testing is warranted. Models and prevalence data are most effective when they are applied to the populations that are best suited for their use. For instance, a model or prevalence data derived from a population study of individuals older than 35 years may not accurately be applied in a population aged 35 years and younger. Care must be taken when interpreting the data obtained from various risk models because they differ with regard to what is actually being estimated. Some models estimate the risk of a pathogenic variant being present in the family; others estimate the risk of a pathogenic variant being present in the individual being counseled. Some models estimate the risk of specific cancers developing in an individual, while others estimate more than one of the data above. Other important considerations include critical family constructs, which can significantly impact model reliability, such as small family size or male-dominated families when the cancer risks are predominantly female in origin, adoption, and early deaths from other causes.[<a href="#cit/section_5.44">

238144</a>

2382,<a href="#cit/section_5.52">

238352</a>

2384] In addition, most models provide gene and/or syndrome-specific probabilities but do not account for the possibility that the personal and/or family history of cancer may be conferred by an as-yet-unidentified cancer susceptibility gene.[<a href="#cit/section_5.45">

238545</a>

2386] In the absence of a documented pathogenic variant in the family, critical assessment of the personal and family history is essential in determining the usefulness and limitations of probability estimates used to aid in the decisions regarding indications for genetic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_5.45">

238745</a>

2388,<a href="#cit/section_5.51">

238951</a>

2390,<a href="#cit/section_5.52">

239152</a>

2392] </p>

2393<p id="_1922" tabindex="-1">

2394For more information on syndrome-specific models, see the following PDQ summaries:</p>

2395<div class="pdq-content-list">

2396<ul id="_1917">

2397<li>

2398<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq">

2399Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers</a>

2400</li>

2401<li>

2402<a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_12">

2403Genetics of Colorectal Cancer</a>

2404</li>

2405<li>

2406<a href="/types/skin/hp/skin-genetics-pdq#_390">

2407Genetics of Skin Cancer</a>

2408</li>

2409</ul>

2410</div>

2411<p id="_293" tabindex="-1">

2412When a pathogenic variant has been identified in a family and a test report documents that finding, prior probabilities can be ascertained with a greater degree of reliability. In this setting, probabilities can be calculated based on the pattern of inheritance associated with the gene in which the pathogenic variant has been identified. In addition, critical to the application of mendelian inheritance is the consideration of integrating Bayes Theorem, which incorporates other variables, such as current age, into the calculation for a more accurate posterior probability.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

24131</a>

2414,<a href="#cit/section_5.53">

241553</a>

2416] This is especially useful in individuals who have lived to be older than the age at which cancer is likely to develop based on the pathogenic variant identified in their family and therefore have a lower likelihood of harboring the family pathogenic variant when compared with the probability based on their relationship to the carrier in the family.</p>

2417<p id="_294" tabindex="-1">

2418Even in the case of a documented pathogenic variant on one side of the family, careful assessment and evaluation of the individual&#8217;s personal and family history of cancer is essential to rule out cancer risk or suspicion of a cancer susceptibility gene pathogenic variant on the other side of the family (maternal or paternal, as applicable).[<a href="#cit/section_5.54">

241954</a>

2420] Segregation of more than one pathogenic variant in a family is possible (e.g., in circumstances in which a cancer syndrome has founder pathogenic variants associated with families of particular ancestral origin).</p>

2421</section>

2422<section id="_200">

2423<h5 id="_200_toc">

2424Risk of developing cancer</h5>

2425<p id="_201" tabindex="-1">

2426Unlike pathogenic variant probability models that predict the likelihood that a given personal and/or family history of cancer could be associated with a pathogenic variant in a specific gene(s), other methods and models can be used to estimate the risk of developing cancer over time. Similar to pathogenic variant probability assessments, cancer risk calculations are also complex and necessitate a detailed health history and family history. In the presence of a documented pathogenic variant, cancer risk estimates can be derived from peer-reviewed penetrance data.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

24271</a>

2428] Penetrance data are constantly being refined and many genetic variants have variable penetrance because other variables may impact the absolute risk of cancer in any given patient. Modifiers of cancer risk in carriers of pathogenic variants include the variant's effect on the function of the gene/protein (e.g., variant type and position), the contributions of modifier genes, and personal and environmental factors (e.g., the impact of bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy performed for other indications in a woman who harbors a <em class="gene-name">

2429BRCA</em>

2430 pathogenic variant).[<a href="#cit/section_5.55">

243155</a>

2432] When there is evidence of an inherited susceptibility to cancer but genetic testing has not been performed, analysis of the pedigree can be used to estimate cancer risk. This type of calculation uses the probability the individual harbors a genetic variant and variant-specific penetrance data to calculate cancer risk.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

24331</a>

2434]</p>

2435<p id="_295" tabindex="-1">

2436In the absence of evidence of a hereditary cancer syndrome, several methods can be utilized to estimate cancer risk. Relative risk data from studies of specific risk factors provide ratios of observed versus expected cancers associated with a given risk factor. However, utilizing relative risk data for individualized risk assessment can have significant limitations: relative risk calculations will differ based on the type of control group and other study-associated biases, and comparability across studies can vary widely.[<a href="#cit/section_5.53">

243753</a>

2438] In addition, relative risks are lifetime ratios and do not provide age-specific calculations, nor can the relative risk be multiplied by <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460205&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460205&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2439population risk</a>

2440 to provide an individual's risk estimate.[<a href="#cit/section_5.53">

244153</a>

2442,<a href="#cit/section_5.56">

244356</a>

2444]</p>

2445<p id="_296" tabindex="-1">

2446In spite of these limitations, disease-specific cumulative risk estimates are most often employed in clinical settings. These estimates usually provide risk for a given time interval and can be anchored to cumulative risks of other health conditions in a given population (e.g., the 5-year risk by the Gail model).[<a href="#cit/section_5.53">

244753</a>

2448,<a href="#cit/section_5.56">

244956</a>

2450] Cumulative risk models have limitations that may underestimate or overestimate risk. For example, the Gail model excludes paternal family histories of breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.52">

245152</a>

2452] Furthermore, many of these models were constructed from data derived from predominantly white populations and may have limited validity when used to estimate risk in other ethnicities.[<a href="#cit/section_5.57">

245357</a>

2454]</p>

2455<p id="_297" tabindex="-1">

2456Cumulative risk estimates are best used when evidence of other underlying significant risk factors have been ruled out. Careful evaluation of an individual's personal health and family history can identify other confounding risk factors that may outweigh a risk estimate derived from a cumulative risk model. For example, a woman with a prior biopsy showing lobular carcinoma <em>

2457in situ</em>

2458 (LCIS) whose mother was diagnosed with breast cancer at age 65 years has a greater lifetime risk from her history of LCIS than her cumulative lifetime risk of breast cancer based on one first-degree relative.[<a href="#cit/section_5.58">

245958</a>

2460,<a href="#cit/section_5.59">

246159</a>

2462] In this circumstance, recommendations for cancer risk management would be based on the risk associated with her LCIS. Unfortunately, there is no reliable method for combining all of an individual's relevant risk factors for an accurate absolute cancer risk estimate, nor are individual risk factors additive.</p>

2463<p id="_298" tabindex="-1">

2464In summary, careful ascertainment and review of personal health and cancer family history are essential adjuncts to the use of prior probability models and cancer risk assessment models to assure that critical elements influencing risk calculations are considered.[<a href="#cit/section_5.51">

246551</a>

2466] Influencing factors include the following: </p>

2467<div class="pdq-content-list">

2468<ul id="_348">

2469<li>

2470Differential diagnosis that is consistent with the personal and cancer family history.</li>

2471<li>

2472Consideration of factors that influence how informative the family history may be.</li>

2473<li>

2474Population that is best suited for the use of the model.</li>

2475<li>

2476Tumor-specific features that may be suspicious for an inherited predisposition or modify individual cancer risk predictions.</li>

2477<li>

2478Model-specific limitations that can overestimate or underestimate calculations.[<a href="#cit/section_5.45">

247945</a>

2480] </li>

2481</ul>

2482</div>

2483<p id="_349" tabindex="-1">

2484A number of investigators are developing health care provider decision support tools such as the Genetic Risk Assessment on the Internet with Decision Support (GRAIDS),[<a href="#cit/section_5.60">

248560</a>

2486] but at this time, clinical judgment remains a key component of any prior probability or absolute cancer risk estimation.[<a href="#cit/section_5.51">

248751</a>

2488]</p>

2489</section>

2490</section>

2491</section>
