2855<section id="_78">

2856<h3 id="_78_toc">

2857Factors to Consider When Offering Testing</h3>

2858<section id="_304">

2859<h4 id="_304_toc">

2860Indications for testing</h4>

2861<p id="_80" tabindex="-1">

2862Experts recommend offering genetic testing when a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460214&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460214&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2863risk assessment</a>

2864

2865suggests the presence of an <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339343&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339343&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2866inherited cancer syndrome</a>

2867 for which specific

2868<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000045693&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2869genes</a>

2870 have been identified. The American Society of Clinical Oncology (ASCO)

2871Policy Statement on Genetic Testing for Cancer Susceptibility proposes that genetic testing be offered when the following conditions apply:[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

28721</a>

2873,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

28742</a>

2875]</p>

2876<div class="pdq-content-list">

2877<ul id="_81">

2878<li>

2879An individual has a personal or family history suggestive of a genetic cancer susceptibility syndrome.</li>

2880<li>

2881The results of the test can be interpreted.</li>

2882<li>

2883Testing will influence medical management.</li>

2884</ul>

2885</div>

2886<p id="_82" tabindex="-1">

2887Characteristics used in making this determination are

2888discussed in the PDQ summaries on the genetics of specific cancers. Even when individual and family history

2889characteristics indicate a possible inherited cancer syndrome, individuals may

2890elect not to proceed with testing after discussion of potential risks, benefits, and limitations, as discussed below. Conversely, individuals whose <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2891pedigrees</a>

2892 are

2893incomplete or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=556485&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000556485&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2894uninformative</a>

2895 due to very small family size, early deaths, or

2896incomplete data on key family members may elect to pursue genetic testing in

2897an attempt to better define their risk status. In these situations, it is

2898particularly important that the pretest counseling fully explore the

2899limitations of the testing process.

2900</p>

2901<p id="_1174" tabindex="-1">

2902ASCO's 2010 and 2015 policy statements addressed testing for low- to moderate-<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339344&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339344&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2903penetrance</a>

2904 genes and direct-to-consumer testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

29051</a>

2906,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

29072</a>

2908]</p>

2909<p id="_1175" tabindex="-1">

2910ASCO&#8217;s position is that when a test, regardless of clinical utility, is ordered by a health care professional, the provider is responsible for organizing follow-up care based on the findings. For tests that are ordered by the consumer without health care professional involvement, management decisions are based on the evidence for clinical utility. For tests with accepted clinical utility, follow-up care can be guided by the evidence for cancer risk associated with the genetic test finding. However, in tests ordered by the consumer that have uncertain clinical utility, ASCO recommends that follow-up care consist of education regarding the lack of evidence regarding the test's clinical utility and that cancer risk management decisions be guided by established cancer risk factors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

29111</a>

2912,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

29132</a>

2914]</p>

2915<p id="_1467" tabindex="-1">

2916In 2015, ASCO updated its policy to address the challenges of new technologies in cancer genetics, including <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=775581&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000775581&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2917multigene (panel) testing</a>

2918 for cancer genetic susceptibility, as well as incidental <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2919germline</a>

2920 findings from somatic <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46063&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000046063&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2921mutation</a>

2922 profiling.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

29232</a>

2924] ASCO's statement supports informing patients about medically relevant germline findings discovered during somatic mutation profiling.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

29252</a>

2926]</p>

2927<p id="_305" tabindex="-1">

2928Genetic education and <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2929counseling</a>

2930 approaches (including the interpretation of genetic test results) will vary if genetic testing has already been attempted (for more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1095">

2931Figure 2</a>

2932). In general, there are two primary circumstances in which genetic testing is performed:

2933</p>

2934<div class="pdq-content-list">

2935<ul id="_306">

2936<li>

2937Families with evidence of an inherited susceptibility that have not had any genetic testing or in which genetic testing has not identified a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2938pathogenic variant</a>

2939.</li>

2940<li>

2941Families with a documented pathogenic variant.</li>

2942</ul>

2943</div>

2944<figure id="figure_1095" class="image-center">

2945<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/586615.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

2946Enlarge</a>

2947<img id="_1095" alt="Flowchart showing a multi-step genetic testing algorithm for testing for cancer susceptibility." title="Flowchart showing a multi-step genetic testing algorithm for testing for cancer susceptibility." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/586615-750.jpg">

2948<figcaption class="caption-container">

2949Figure 2. This genetic testing algorithm depicts the multistep process of testing for cancer susceptibility.</figcaption>

2950</figure>

2951</section>

2952<section id="_313">

2953<h4 id="_313_toc">

2954Value of testing an affected family member first</h4>

2955<p id="_84" tabindex="-1">

2956<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=256553&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000256553&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2957Genetic susceptibility</a>

2958 testing generally yields the most useful information

2959when a living family member with the cancer of concern is tested before other family members; this can help determine if his/her cancer has a genetic origin. If testing is deferred while follow-up with an affected relative is pending, consider providing interim cancer risk management guidelines to the unaffected <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460211&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460211&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2960proband</a>

2961.[<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

29623</a>

2963] Possible genetic testing outcomes include the following (for more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1095">

2964Figure 2</a>

2965):</p>

2966<div class="pdq-content-list">

2967<ul id="_314">

2968<li>

2969Pathogenic variant detected.</li>

2970<li>

2971No variant detected.</li>

2972<li>

2973<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=556495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000556495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2974Variant of uncertain significance</a>

2975 (VUS) detected.</li>

2976</ul>

2977</div>

2978<p id="_1099" tabindex="-1">

2979If a documented pathogenic variant (associated with cancer risk) is

2980identified, risks are based on penetrance data for pathogenic variants of that specific gene. In addition, other family members may be tested

2981for the presence or absence of this specific pathogenic variant. If no variant is found

2982in an affected family member, testing is considered uninformative and thus there is no basis

2983for testing <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460224&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460224&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2984unaffected</a>

2985 relatives.

2986Failure of the laboratory to detect a pathogenic variant in an affected family member does not rule out an inherited basis for the cancer in that family. Reasons why testing could be uninformative include the following:</p>

2987<div class="pdq-content-list">

2988<ul id="_315">

2989<li>

2990The cancer in the family may be associated with a cancer <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460209&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460209&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2991susceptibility gene</a>

2992 other than the gene that was tested.</li>

2993<li>

2994The cancer in the family may be associated with a pathogenic variant, but the cancer in the specific family member who underwent testing is not associated with that variant. This can occur especially with cancers that are common in the general population, such as breast cancer or prostate cancer. The family member who is affected with the disease but is not a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460132&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460132&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2995carrier</a>

2996 of the pathogenic variant associated with the inherited predisposition to cancer in the family is considered a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=538227&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000538227&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

2997phenocopy</a>

2998.</li>

2999<li>

3000Identifying a genetic variant may not be possible given the limited <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=322883&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000322883&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3001sensitivity</a>

3002 of the laboratory techniques used to detect genetic variants. There may be additional testing available to detect certain types of variants that would have been missed by the initial genetic test.</li>

3003<li>

3004The function of the gene could be altered by a pathogenic variant in a different gene.</li>

3005</ul>

3006</div>

3007<p id="_316" tabindex="-1">

3008Lastly, testing may reveal a VUS. This result means that a genetic variant has been found; however, the extent that this variant increases cancer risk, or whether it is associated with the history of cancer in the family, is uncertain. In this circumstance, some clues as to the significance of the variant can be derived from the following:</p>

3009<div class="pdq-content-list">

3010<ul id="_1093">

3011<li>

3012The location of the variant in relation to regions and function of a gene.</li>

3013<li>

3014The specific change; since many variants are <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783964&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783964&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3015missense variants</a>

3016, not all amino acid substitutions are as significant.</li>

3017<li>

3018Whether the variant has been documented in the presence of a documented pathogenic variant.</li>

3019<li>

3020Whether the variant is associated with the branch in the family with the cancer and/or whether the variant tracks with the cancers in the family.</li>

3021</ul>

3022</div>

3023<p id="_1094" tabindex="-1">

3024Unfortunately, even with this information, there is often insufficient evidence to document the significance of a specific variant, and further clarifying research is required.</p>

3025<p id="_85" tabindex="-1">

3026If there is no close, living, affected relative to undergo testing, or the living affected relative declines testing, other options may be

3027discussed with the patient and the testing laboratory. In rare instances, if proper authorization is secured from the family, testing the stored tissue of a deceased relative may be considered. However, genetic tests done on stored tissue are technically difficult and may not yield a definitive result. Therefore, testing an

3028unaffected person without prior testing of an affected family member may be performed. In these instances, counseling includes discussing that a negative test result does not rule out the

3029presence of a cancer susceptibility gene in the family or in the patient and may be uninformative. </p>

3030</section>

3031<section id="_307">

3032<h4 id="_307_toc">

3033Testing in families with a documented pathogenic variant</h4>

3034<p id="_308" tabindex="-1">

3035Genetic testing for a documented familial pathogenic variant can be <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460156&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460156&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3036informative</a>

3037 and will yield one of the following results (for more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1095">

3038Figure 2</a>

3039):</p>

3040<div class="pdq-content-list">

3041<ul id="_309">

3042<li>

3043Positive for the familial pathogenic variant.</li>

3044<li>

3045Negative for the familial pathogenic variant.</li>

3046</ul>

3047</div>

3048<p id="_310" tabindex="-1">

3049If the familial pathogenic variant is detected in a family member, their cancer risks are based on penetrance data for pathogenic variants in that specific gene. If the documented pathogenic variant is not found in a family member, the risk of cancer in that individual is equivalent to cancer risk in the general population. However, other risk factors and family history from the side of the family not associated with the documented pathogenic variant may increase the cancer risk above the general population levels.</p>

3050<p id="_317" tabindex="-1">

3051In summary, genetic education and counseling includes identifying the most informative person in the family to test, which may be an affected family member rather than the individual seeking genetic services. In addition, counseling includes a discussion of the limitations of the test, all possible test outcomes, and the consequences of identifying a VUS.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

30524</a>

3053]</p>

3054</section>

3055<section id="_1464">

3056<h4 id="_1464_toc">

3057Insurance coverage</h4>

3058<p id="_1481" tabindex="-1">

3059Insurance coverage varies for cancer susceptibility testing, including multigene (panel) testing. In general, most individuals who meet specific criteria (e.g., National Comprehensive Cancer Network [NCCN] guidelines for <em class="gene-name">

3060BRCA1</em>

3061/<em class="gene-name">

3062BRCA2</em>

3063 or Lynch syndrome testing) are able to obtain insurance coverage for multigene testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

30645</a>

3065] Of note, some insurance companies have contracts with specific laboratories through which testing must be ordered. </p>

3066<p id="_1465" tabindex="-1">

3067The Affordable Care Act (ACA) requires that private insurers cover&#8212;with no out-of-pocket costs to the insured&#8212;genetic counseling and <em class="gene-name">

3068BRCA1</em>

3069/<em class="gene-name">

3070BRCA2</em>

3071 testing for unaffected women meeting United States Preventive Services Task Force guidelines.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

30726</a>

3073,<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

30747</a>

3075] Importantly, under ACA guidelines, women with a prior cancer diagnosis are not covered. The ACA does not stipulate that follow-up care based on genetic test results be covered (e.g., risk-reducing surgeries). However, some insurance companies require that pretest genetic counseling be performed by a credentialed genetics provider before testing is authorized. Before testing is ordered, it is important to verify costs and insurance coverage, including for Medicaid and Medicare patients. Medicare does not cover genetic testing if the patient has not had a cancer diagnosis associated with the pathogenic variants for which testing is ordered. In addition, unaffected individuals with Medicare are not covered for testing, even if they are tested for only a known familial pathogenic variant. Further, Medicare does not cover genetic counseling as a separately billable service.[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

30768</a>

3077] For individuals without insurance coverage and the underinsured, some laboratories offer low-cost options or have financial assistance programs.</p>

3078</section>

3079<section id="_318">

3080<h4 id="_318_toc">

3081Genetic testing and assisted reproductive technology</h4>

3082<p id="_1368" tabindex="-1">

3083There is a risk of carriers passing on cancer-associated pathogenic variants to offspring. When an individual tests positive for one pathogenic variant in a cancer susceptibility gene, counseling about reproductive implications addresses not only the risks associated with <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=793860&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000793860&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3084autosomal dominant inheritance</a>

3085 but also the potential risks of having a child with two pathogenic variants in the same gene (<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=775789&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000775789&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3086biallelic</a>

3087) that could result in a severe condition.</p>

3088<p id="_1638" tabindex="-1">

3089<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339335&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339335&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3090Assisted reproductive technology</a>

3091 can be used for preimplantation genetic testing (PGT) and for prenatal cancer predisposition genetic testing using chorionic villus sampling and amniocentesis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

30929</a>

3093-<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

309411</a>

3095] For individuals with autosomal dominant cancer syndromes (e.g., those associated with <em class="gene-name">

3096APC</em>

3097, <em class="gene-name">

3098BRCA1</em>

3099/<em class="gene-name">

3100BRCA2</em>

3101, <em class="gene-name">

3102PTEN</em>

3103, or <em class="gene-name">

3104TP53</em>

3105 pathogenic variants), reproductive options exist for prenatal testing and PGT to detect offspring with one copy of the pathogenic variant (<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339341&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339341&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3106heterozygotes</a>

3107). </p>

3108<p id="_1381" tabindex="-1">

3109In some cases (e.g., carriers of germline pathogenic variants in <em class="gene-name">

3110ATM</em>

3111, <em class="gene-name">

3112BLM</em>

3113), assessing an individual&#8217;s partner's risk for carrying a pathogenic variant associated with a dominant or recessive syndrome (i.e., his or her personal and family history and ethnicity) is indicated. In the unlikely event that both parents are heterozygous for specific pathogenic variants, there is a 25% risk that a child will be <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339342&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339342&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3114homozygous</a>

3115 and could have a severe <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460203&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460203&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3116phenotype</a>

3117. In light of this information, couples may consider PGT or prenatal testing.</p>

3118<p id="_285" tabindex="-1">

3119A proposed analytic framework for counseling carriers about reproduction options includes consideration of the following issues:[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

312010</a>

3121]</p>

3122<div class="pdq-content-list">

3123<ol id="_286">

3124<li>

3125Does the cancer syndrome include childhood malignancies or significant morbidity or mortality at an early age?</li>

3126<li>

3127What is the penetrance associated with the genetic variant?</li>

3128<li>

3129How severe is the syndrome phenotype?</li>

3130<li>

3131Are there interventions available that decrease the pathogenic variant-associated cancer risk or are proven to detect cancer early when it is in a treatable form?</li>

3132<li>

3133Is there evidence of a different phenotype if an individual is a heterozygous or homozygous carrier?[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

313412</a>

3135,<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

313613</a>

3137]</li>

3138</ol>

3139</div>

3140<p id="_1382" tabindex="-1">

3141In a study of 320 patients with different <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=754220&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000754220&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3142hereditary cancer syndromes</a>

3143, most were unaware of PGT; however, the majority expressed interest in learning more about the availability of PGT.[<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

314414</a>

3145] Patients also preferred having a discussion about PGT with their genetic counselor or primary physician. Disease-specific factors (e.g., severity of the hereditary condition, quality of life, and medical interventions) and individual factors (e.g., gender, childbearing status, and religious beliefs) affected patient attitudes about PGT.</p>

3146</section>

3147</section>

3148<section id="_86">

3149<h3 id="_86_toc">

3150Determining the Test to Be Used</h3>

3151<p id="_87" tabindex="-1">

3152Genetic testing is highly specialized. There are also multiple molecular testing

3153methods available, each with its own indications, costs, strengths, and weaknesses.

3154Depending on the method employed and the extent of the analysis, different

3155tests for the same gene will have varying levels of sensitivity and

3156<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=322884&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000322884&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3157specificity</a>

3158. Even assuming high analytic validity, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339333&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339333&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3159genetic heterogeneity</a>

3160 makes

3161test selection challenging. A number of different genetic syndromes may

3162underlie the development of a particular cancer type. For example, hereditary colorectal

3163cancer may be due to familial adenomatous polyposis (FAP), Lynch syndrome, Peutz-Jeghers syndrome, juvenile

3164polyposis syndrome, or other syndromes. Each of these has a different

3165genetic basis. In addition, different genes may be responsible for the same

3166condition (e.g., Lynch syndrome can be caused by pathogenic variants in one of several mismatch

3167repair [MMR] genes). </p>

3168<p id="_1209" tabindex="-1">

3169In some genes, the same pathogenic variant has been found in multiple, apparently unrelated families. This observation is consistent with a founder effect, wherein a pathogenic variant identified in a contemporary population can be traced back to a small group of founders isolated by geographic, cultural, or other factors. For example, two specific <em class="gene-name">

3170BRCA1</em>

3171 pathogenic variants (68_69delAG and 5266dup, also known in the literature as 185delAG and 5382insC) and one <em class="gene-name">

3172BRCA2</em>

3173 pathogenic variant (5946delT, also known as 6174delT) have been reported to be common in Ashkenazi Jews. Other genes also have reported <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783962&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783962&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3174founder pathogenic variants</a>

3175. The presence of founder pathogenic variants has practical implications for genetic testing. Many laboratories offer directed testing specifically for ethnic-specific <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339337&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339337&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3176alleles</a>

3177. This greatly simplifies the technical aspects of the test but is not without limitations. For example, approximately 15% of <em class="gene-name">

3178BRCA1</em>

3179 and <em class="gene-name">

3180BRCA2</em>

3181 pathogenic variants that occur among Ashkenazim are nonfounder pathogenic variants.[<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

318215</a>

3183] Also, for genes in which large genome rearrangements are common in the founder population, ordering additional testing using different techniques may be needed. </p>

3184<p id="_1210" tabindex="-1">

3185<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460125&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460125&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3186Allelic heterogeneity</a>

3187 (i.e., different variants

3188within the same gene) can confer different risks or be associated with a

3189different phenotype. For example, though the general rule is that adenomatous

3190polyposis coli (<em class="gene-name">

3191APC</em>

3192) pathogenic variants are associated with hundreds or thousands of

3193colonic polyps and colon cancer of the classical FAP syndrome, some <em class="gene-name">

3194APC</em>

3195

3196pathogenic variants cause a milder clinical picture, with fewer polyps and lower

3197colorectal cancer risk.[<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

319816</a>

3199,<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

320017</a>

3201] In addition, other disorders may be part of the FAP

3202spectrum. Pathogenic variants in a certain portion of the <em class="gene-name">

3203APC</em>

3204 gene also predispose to

3205retinal changes, for example, when pathogenic variants in a different region of <em class="gene-name">

3206APC</em>

3207

3208predispose to desmoid tumors. </p>

3209<p id="_1452" tabindex="-1">

3210Since hereditary cancer syndromes can have varying presentations and overlapping phenotypes, selecting the appropriate genetic test

3211may require knowledge on the following topics:</p>

3212<div class="pdq-content-list">

3213<ul id="_1918">

3214<li>

3215Genetic syndromes.</li>

3216<li>

3217Diagnostic methods used for identifying pathogenic variants.</li>

3218<li>

3219Correlations between clinical and molecular test findings.</li>

3220<li>

3221Information regarding rapidly changing genetic testing options.</li>

3222</ul>

3223</div>

3224<p id="_1924" tabindex="-1">

3225See the following PDQ summaries for more information:</p>

3226<div class="pdq-content-list">

3227<ul id="_1919">

3228<li>

3229<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq">

3230Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers</a>

3231</li>

3232<li>

3233<a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq">

3234Genetics of Colorectal Cancer</a>

3235</li>

3236<li>

3237<a href="/types/thyroid/hp/medullary-thyroid-genetics-pdq">

3238Genetics of Endocrine and Neuroendocrine Neoplasias</a>

3239</li>

3240<li>

3241<a href="/types/skin/hp/skin-genetics-pdq">

3242Genetics of Skin Cancer</a>

3243</li>

3244<li>

3245<a href="/types/kidney/hp/renal-cell-carcinoma-genetics">

3246Genetics of Renal Cell Carcinoma</a>

3250Genetics of Prostate Cancer</a>

3253</div>

3254<section id="_1320">

3255<h4 id="_1320_toc">

3256Multigene (panel) testing</h4>

3257<p id="_1319" tabindex="-1">

3258<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=763024&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000763024&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3259Next-generation sequencing</a>

3260 (NGS) and the removal of most patent barriers to diagnostic DNA sequencing [<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

326118</a>

3262] have resulted in the availability of multigene testing, which can simultaneously test more than 50 genes for pathogenic variants, often at costs comparable to single-gene testing. These multigene panels can include genes with pathogenic variants that are associated with high risks of cancer and genes that confer moderate and uncertain risks. The multigene panels can be limited to specific cancer types (e.g., breast, ovarian, colon) or can include many cancer types. This type of testing has both advantages and disadvantages, and much of the information presented in this section is not based on empirical data but rather on commentaries.</p>

3263<section id="_1333">

3264<h5 id="_1333_toc">

3265Genetic education and counseling for multigene testing</h5>

3266<p id="_1324" tabindex="-1">

3267ASCO has stressed the importance of genetic counseling to ensure patients are adequately informed about the implications of this type of testing and recommends that tests be ordered by cancer genetic professionals.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

32682</a>

3269,<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

327019</a>

3271] Yet, the use of multigene testing requires modification of traditional approaches to genetic counseling.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

327220</a>

3273,<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

327421</a>

3275] Optimal evidence-based counseling strategies have not yet been established. Unlike in-person, single-gene pretest genetic counseling models, these approaches have not been examined for outcomes of counseling such as comprehension, satisfaction, psychosocial outcomes, and testing uptake. <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1425">

3276Table 1</a>

3277 summarizes recommendations from ASCO on elements of pretest genetic counseling and informed consent for germline cancer genetic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

32782</a>

3279]</p>
