3426<section id="_1325">

3427<h5 id="_1325_toc">

3428Research examining multigene testing</h5>

3429<p id="_1409" tabindex="-1">

3430The range of results from NGS multigene panels is emerging in both data from clinical and laboratory series. Several of the studies are collaborations between the two. There are several important caveats about the research that has been conducted so far with regard to multigene testing:</p>

3431<div class="pdq-content-list">

3432<ul id="_1410">

3433<li>

3434The studies differ in their aims, approaches, ascertainment of subjects, and panels used. </li>

3435<li>

3436Laboratory- and clinic-based studies likely differ with regard to their sampling frames (the population a study draws from and its characteristics). For example, some studies may include testing by a wide variety of health care professionals, some of whom may not be as experienced in triaging, testing, and advising high-risk patients.[<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

343722</a>

3438]</li>

3439<li>

3440Testing methodologies also differ among laboratories regarding <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460146&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460146&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3441exon</a>

3442/<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=660737&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000660737&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3443intron</a>

3444 coverage, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=778722&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000778722&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3445read depth</a>

3446, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=763028&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000763028&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3447Sanger sequencing</a>

3448 confirmation, and variant interpretation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

344923</a>

3450]</li>

3451<li>

3452The genes to be tested as part of a multigene panel are constantly changing. In some studies, the composition of multigene panels changed during the course of the study, usually to include more genes.[<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

345324</a>

3454]</li>

3455<li>

3456Some patient populations included a mix of patients already tested by traditional single-gene methods and those undergoing testing for the first time, making it difficult to establish true diagnostic yield.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

345725</a>

3458,<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

345926</a>

3460]</li>

3461<li>

3462In the studies that replicated previous <em class="gene-name">

3463BRCA</em>

3464 testing with a panel, the analytic validity of the NGS multigene panel tests is equivalent to the former single-gene tests, with almost 100% concordance in patients who had both single-gene <em class="gene-name">

3465BRCA</em>

3466 testing and multigene testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

346725</a>

3468,<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

346926</a>

3470]</li>

3471</ul>

3472</div>

3473<p id="_1411" tabindex="-1">

3474In high-risk individuals who meet criteria for hereditary cancer genetic testing but in whom no pathogenic variant was identified from single-gene testing, panel testing may identify other clinically actionable variants.[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

347527</a>

3476,<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

347728</a>

3478] For example, the additional yield of multigene testing in individuals in whom a <em class="gene-name">

3479BRCA1</em>

3480/<em class="gene-name">

3481BRCA2</em>

3482 pathogenic variant was not detected currently seems to be approximately 4%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

348326</a>

3484,<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

348529</a>

3486,<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

348730</a>

3488] The most common non-<em class="gene-name">

3489BRCA</em>

3490 pathogenic variants found are in <em class="gene-name">

3491CHEK2</em>

3492, <em class="gene-name">

3493ATM</em>

3494, and <em class="gene-name">

3495PALB2</em>

3496.[<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

349726</a>

3498,<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

349929</a>

3500-<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

350131</a>

3502] In some cases, the identification of pathogenic variants from panel testing resulted in additional recommendations for <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000046171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3503screening</a>

3504 and risk reduction beyond what would have been indicated based on family history alone.[<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

350530</a>

3506,<a href="#cit/section_6.32">

350732</a>

3508-<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

350934</a>

3510]</p>

3511<p id="_1595" tabindex="-1">

3512Selected reports from 2014 to 2018, which included 1,000 to 10,000 tested individuals, showed variation in pathogenic variant and VUS rates.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

351323</a>

3514,<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

351524</a>

3516,<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

351726</a>

3518,<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

351930</a>

3520,<a href="#cit/section_6.35">

352135</a>

3522-<a href="#cit/section_6.38">

352338</a>

3524] Pathogenic variant rates ranged from 7% to 14%; VUS rates ranged from 19% to 41% and increased with the number of genes included on the panel, but decreased in the later studies, likely because of larger data pools and refinements in variant interpretation. Additionally, VUS rates were higher in non-White individuals, likely because of the limited availability of test result data needed for accurate determination of risk.[<a href="#cit/section_6.38">

352538</a>

3526]</p>

3527<p id="_1596" tabindex="-1">

3528A large study published by a commercial laboratory included more than 252,000 individuals who were tested with a 25-gene panel between 2013 and 2016.[<a href="#cit/section_6.39">

352939</a>

3530] The study population did not have prior cancer genetic testing, 97% were female, and 93% met NCCN criteria for hereditary breast and ovarian cancer (HBOC) or Lynch syndrome testing. Half of the pathogenic variants found for HBOC or Lynch syndrome were not in the expected genes associated with these syndromes (<em class="gene-name">

3531BRCA1</em>

3532, <em class="gene-name">

3533BRCA2</em>

3534, <em class="gene-name">

3535MLH1</em>

3536, <em class="gene-name">

3537MSH2</em>

3538, <em class="gene-name">

3539MSH6</em>

3540, and <em class="gene-name">

3541PMS2</em>

3542).</p>

3543</section>

3544<section id="_1334">

3545<h5 id="_1334_toc">

3546Outcomes of multigene testing</h5>

3547<p id="_1335" tabindex="-1">

3548Results from multigene tests have several possible outcomes, including the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

354919</a>

3550]</p>

3551<div class="pdq-content-list">

3552<ul id="_1348">

3553<li>

3554No variant detected.</li>

3555<li>

3556 VUS detected.</li>

3557<li>

3558Pathogenic variant in a high-penetrance gene concordant with the existing personal/family history (e.g., a germline <em class="gene-name">

3559<a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_1118">

3560MSH2</a>

3561</em>

3562 pathogenic variant in an individual who meets Amsterdam criteria for Lynch syndrome).</li>

3563<li>

3564Pathogenic variant in a high-penetrance gene discordant with the existing personal/family history (e.g., a germline <em class="gene-name">

3565<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_3780">

3566CDH1</a>

3567</em>

3568 pathogenic variant in an individual with no personal/family history of gastric cancer).</li>

3569<li>

3570Pathogenic variant in a moderate-penetrance gene (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

3571<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_1309">

3572CHEK2</a>

3573</em>

3574, <em class="gene-name">

3575<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_1311">

3576ATM</a>

3577</em>

3578).</li>

3579<li>

3580Pathogenic variant in a gene with uncertain cancer risks and/or cancer associations.</li>

3581</ul>

3582</div>

3583<p id="_1349" tabindex="-1">

3584Results can also reveal more than one finding given that multiple genes are being tested simultaneously and the elevated rate of VUS.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

358521</a>

3586] There has been no assessment of outcomes of multigene tests such as comprehension, psychosocial outcomes, and uptake of cancer risk management options. </p>

3587</section>

3588<section id="_1323">

3589<h5 id="_1323_toc">

3590Considerations when using multigene testing</h5>

3591<p id="_1331" tabindex="-1">

3592Using multigene panels can be complex. However, this approach may offer advantages over sequential testing strategies. For example, in some types of cancer, several genes can be associated with specific phenotypes; therefore, testing for all genes associated with a given phenotype can save both time and money.[<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

359340</a>

3594] Additionally, multigene panel testing may identify the genetic basis of cancer in families with the following: a differential diagnosis list that includes multiple syndromes, or a family history that does not meet genetic testing criteria for a hereditary cancer syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

359521</a>

3596,<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

359740</a>

3598] For more information on factors that make family history difficult to interpret, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_174">

3599Analysis of the family history</a>

3600 section.</p>

3601<p id="_1376" tabindex="-1">

3602However, there can be challenges when employing this testing approach. Clinical laboratories now offer a varying array of clinical cancer susceptibility gene panels.[<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

360341</a>

3604,<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

360542</a>

3606] Multigene panels continue to evolve, and the genes included on the panels can change. Other challenges of interpreting multigene test results include higher rates of VUS than those seen in single-gene testing (the rate of VUS increases with the number of genes tested),[<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

360724</a>

3608] higher rates of VUS in some racial and ethnic minority populations,[<a href="#cit/section_6.32">

360932</a>

3610,<a href="#cit/section_6.43">

361143</a>

3612] and the detection of variants in genes associated with uncertain cancer risks. </p>

3613<p id="_1377" tabindex="-1">

3614Providers who determine a patient's genetic testing strategy may also consider these additional challenges: the patient's out-of-pocket and overall expenses, insurance reimbursement for the genetic test, the genetic test's turn-around-time, ease of ordering the genetic test from a laboratory, the probability of identifying a VUS and managing this finding (via VUS reclassification and access to additional variant data), technical differences between genetic tests (such as the presence of a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460141&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460141&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3615deletion</a>

3616/duplication assay), patient preference, and a patient's clinical history.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

36172</a>

3618,<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

361940</a>

3620,<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

362141</a>

3622,<a href="#cit/section_6.44">

362344</a>

3624]</p>

3625<p id="_1482" tabindex="-1">

3626Practice guidelines for optimal clinical use of multigene tests continue to evolve.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

36272</a>

3628,<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

362945</a>

3630] The NCCN and ASCO guidelines suggest that multigene panel testing may be more efficient when there are multiple cancer syndromes or genes on the differential diagnosis list.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

36312</a>

3632,<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

363345</a>

3634] Additionally, NCCN states that there may be a role for multigene panel testing when a patient has a personal or family history of cancer that is consistent with an inherited susceptibility, but single-gene testing has not identified a pathogenic variant.[<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

363545</a>

3636]</p>

3637<p id="_1483" tabindex="-1">

3638Another important consideration is that multigene tests may include genes in which pathogenic variants are associated with moderate or uncertain penetrance. Management of individuals with pathogenic variants in such genes can present additional challenges, particularly when expert consensus or evidence-based recommendations are not available. For more information on moderate- and low-penetrance, see <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/overview-pdq#_2583">

3639Figure 1</a>

3640 in Cancer Genetics Overview. Moreover, there may be limited or no evidence to support changes to medical management based on the level of risk or uncertain risk; however, management may still be affected by family history.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

36411</a>

3642,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

36432</a>

3644] A framework for clinical management incorporates emerging data on age-specific, lifetime, and absolute cancer risks conferred by pathogenic variants in several moderate-risk genes.[<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

364546</a>

3646] For more information about frameworks for clinical management, see the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_2730">

3647Penetrance of Inherited Susceptibility to Hereditary Breast and/or Gynecologic Cancers</a>

3648 section in Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers.</p>

3649</section>

3650</section>

3651<section id="_220">

3652<h4 id="_220_toc">

3653Regulation of genetic tests</h4>

3654<p id="_221" tabindex="-1">

3655Government regulation of genetic tests to date remains extremely limited in terms of both analytic and clinical validity with little interagency coordination.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

365647</a>

3657] The Centers for Medicare &amp; Medicaid Services, using the <a href="https://www.cms.gov/Regulations-and-Guidance/Legislation/CLIA/index?redirect=/CLIA/09_CLIA_Regulations_and_Federal_Register_Documents.asp#TopOfPage" title="https://www.cms.gov/Regulations-and-Guidance/Legislation/CLIA/index?redirect=/CLIA/09_CLIA_Regulations_and_Federal_Register_Documents.asp#TopOfPage">

3658Clinical Laboratory Improvement Act</a>

3659 (CLIA), regulates all clinical human laboratory testing performed in the United States for the purposes of generating diagnostic or other health information. CLIA regulations address personnel qualifications, laboratory quality assurance standards, and documentation and validation of tests and procedures.[<a href="#cit/section_6.48">

366048</a>

3661] For laboratory tests themselves, CLIA categorizes tests based on the level of complexity into waived tests, moderate complexity, or high complexity. Genetic tests are considered high complexity, which indicates that a high degree of knowledge and skill is required to perform or interpret the test. Laboratories conducting high complexity tests must undergo proficiency testing at specified intervals, which consists of an external review of the laboratory's ability to accurately perform and interpret the test.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

366247</a>

3663,<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

366449</a>

3665] However, a specialty area specific for molecular and biologic genetic tests has yet to be established; therefore, specific proficiency testing of genetic testing laboratories is not required by CLIA.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

366647</a>

3667]</p>

3668<p id="_288" tabindex="-1">

3669In regard to analytic validity, genetic tests fall into two primary categories; test kits and laboratory-developed tests (previously called home brews). Test kits are manufactured for use in laboratories performing the test and include all the reagents necessary to complete the analysis, instructions, performance outcomes, and details about which genetic variants can be detected. The U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) regulates test kits as medical devices; however, despite more than 1,000 available genetic tests, there are fewer than ten FDA-approved test kits.[<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

367049</a>

3671] Laboratory-developed tests are performed in a laboratory that assembles its own testing materials in-house;[<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

367249</a>

3673] this category represents the most common form of genetic testing. Laboratory-developed tests are subject to the least amount of oversight, as neither CLIA nor the FDA evaluate the laboratories' proficiency in performing the test or clinical validity relative to the accuracy of the test to predict a clinical outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

367447</a>

3675,<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

367649</a>

3677] The FDA does regulate manufactured analyte-specific reagents (ASRs) as medical devices. These small molecules are used to conduct laboratory-developed tests but can also be made by the laboratory. ASRs made in the laboratory are not subject to FDA oversight. For laboratory-developed tests utilizing manufactured commercially available ASRs, the FDA requires that the test be ordered by a health professional or other individual authorized to order the test by state law. However, this regulation does not distinguish between health providers caring for the patient or health providers who work for the laboratory offering the test.[<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

367849</a>

3679]</p>

3680<p id="_289" tabindex="-1">

3681In addition to the regulation of classical clinical genetic tests is the regulatory oversight of research genetic testing. Laboratories performing genetic testing on a research basis are exempt from CLIA oversight if the laboratory does not report patient-specific results for the diagnosis, prevention, or treatment of any disease or impairment or the assessment of the health of individual patients.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

368247</a>

3683] However, there are anecdotal reports of research laboratories providing test results for clinical purposes with the caveat that the laboratory recommends that testing be repeated in a clinical CLIA-approved laboratory. In addition, there is no established mechanism that determines when a test has sufficient analytic and clinical validity to be offered clinically.[<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

368449</a>

3685] Currently, the decision to offer a genetic test clinically is at the discretion of the laboratory director.</p>

3686<p id="_290" tabindex="-1">

3687Evidence regarding the implications of this narrow regulatory oversight of genetic tests is limited and consists predominantly of laboratory director responses to quality assurance surveys. A survey of 133 laboratory directors performing genetic tests found that 88% of laboratories employed one or more American Board of Medical Genetics (ABMG)-certified or ABMG-eligible professional geneticists, and 23% had an affiliation with at least one doctoral-prepared geneticist. Eight percent of laboratories did not employ and were not affiliated with doctoral-level genetics professionals. Laboratory-developed tests were performed in 70% of laboratories. Sixty-three percent of laboratories provided an interpretation of the test result as part of the test report.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

368850</a>

3689] Another survey of 190 laboratory directors found that 97% were CLIA-certified for high complexity testing. Sixteen percent of laboratories reported no specialty area certification; those without specialty certification represented laboratories with the most volume of tests performed and offered the most extensive test selection.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

369047</a>

3691] Of laboratories with specialty certification, not all had certification relevant to genetic tests, with 48% reporting pathology certification, 46% chemistry certification, and 41% clinical <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270737&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000270737&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3692cytogenetics</a>

3693 certification. Sixteen percent of directors reported participation in no formal external proficiency testing program, although 77% performed some informal proficiency testing when a formal external proficiency testing program was not available. </p>

3694<p id="_350" tabindex="-1">

3695The most frequent reason cited for lack of proficiency testing participation was lack of available proficiency testing programs. Laboratory directors estimated that in the past 2 years 37% issued three or fewer incorrect reports, and 35% issued at least four incorrect reports. Analytic errors such as faulty reagent, equipment failure, or human error, increased 40% with each decrease in level of proficiency training completed.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

369647</a>

3697] An international genetic testing laboratory director survey involving 18 countries found that 64% of the 827 laboratories that responded accepted samples from outside their country.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

369851</a>

3699] Similar to the U.S. study, 74% reported participation in some form of proficiency testing. Fifty-three percent of the laboratories required a copy of the consent to perform the test, and 72% of laboratories retained specimens indefinitely that were submitted for testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

370051</a>

3701]</p>

3702<p id="_1089" tabindex="-1">

3703The U.S. Department of Health and Human Services Secretary&#8217;s Advisory Committee on Genetics, Health, and Society has published a detailed report regarding the adequacy and transparency of the current oversight system for genetic testing in the United States.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

370452</a>

3705] The Committee identified gaps in the following areas:</p>

3706<div class="pdq-content-list">

3707<ul id="_1090">

3708<li>

3709Regulations governing clinical laboratory quality.</li>

3710<li>

3711Oversight of the clinical validity of genetic tests.</li>

3712<li>

3713The number and identification of laboratories performing genetic tests and the specific genetic tests being performed.</li>

3714<li>

3715Level of current knowledge about the clinical usefulness of genetic tests.</li>

3716<li>

3717Educational preparation in genetics of health providers, the public health community, patients, and consumers.</li>

3718</ul>

3719</div>

3720<p id="_1468" tabindex="-1">

3721In October 2014, the FDA posted the notification regarding its plans to develop draft guidance on the regulation of laboratory-developed tests.[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

372253</a>

3723] Draft guidance documents outlining the framework for regulatory oversight for the industry and clinical laboratories were published later in 2014 for public review and comment. Given the potential of such regulatory action to affect the wide spectrum of genetic tests in clinical practice, proposed draft guidelines have been discussed and reviewed by a number of professional associations, eliciting policy statements and analyses from various professional associations, including the <a href="https://www.ashg.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/02/PS-201502.pdf" title="https://www.ashg.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/02/PS-201502.pdf">

3724American Society of Human Genetics (ASHG)</a>

3725 and the <a href="https://www.amp.org/AMP/assets/File/position-statements/2015/AMPResponseFDACaseReportFinal.pdf?pass=48?" title="https://www.amp.org/AMP/assets/File/position-statements/2015/AMPResponseFDACaseReportFinal.pdf?pass=48?">

3726Association for Molecular Pathology</a>

3727. The issue of FDA oversight of laboratory-developed tests remains under consideration.</p>

3728</section>

3729</section>

3730<section id="_362">

3731<h3 id="_362_toc">

3732Direct-to-Consumer (DTC) Genetic Tests</h3>

3733<p id="_1439" tabindex="-1">

3734Most genetic testing for cancer and other health risks is offered by health care providers on the basis of a patient&#8217;s personal history, family history, or ethnicity. Increasingly, however, individuals can order genetic testing through DTC companies without the input of health care providers. DTC tests may provide information about ancestry, paternity, propensity toward certain physical traits, risk of adverse drug reactions, and disease risks. </p>

3735<section id="_1556">

3736<h4 id="_1556_toc">

3737Genotyping for carrier status and disease risks</h4>

3738<p id="_1557" tabindex="-1">

3739In 2015, the FDA provided <a href="https://wayback.archive-it.org/7993/20170722112110/https://www.fda.gov/NewsEvents/Newsroom/PressAnnouncements/ucm435003.htm" title="https://wayback.archive-it.org/7993/20170722112110/https://www.fda.gov/NewsEvents/Newsroom/PressAnnouncements/ucm435003.htm">

3740clearance</a>

3741 for a large DTC company (23andMe) to market carrier screening for Bloom syndrome, which is associated with increased cancer risks in homozygotes as well as other phenotypic features. Subsequently, DTC carrier testing for several conditions became available. In 2017, the FDA allowed 23andMe to market DTC tests for ten diseases or conditions including late-onset Alzheimer disease, Parkinson disease, and hereditary thrombophilia.[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

374254</a>

3743]

3744It is important to note that the carrier and health tests authorized for marketing by the FDA are performed by <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=793548&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000793548&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3745genotyping</a>

3746, which means that only specific <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460201&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460201&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3747nucleotides</a>

3748 or bases are targeted for analysis; sequencing is not performed.[<a href="#cit/section_6.55">

374955</a>

3750] Thus, while the <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460147&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460147&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3751false-positive</a>

3752 or false-negative rate for a specific genotype is very low (i.e., analytic validity is high), other pathogenic variants are not analyzed, nor is the entire sequence of the gene. Thus, the false-negative rate due to untested pathogenic variants as well as other gene abnormalities is high.</p>

3753</section>

3754<section id="_1558">

3755<h4 id="_1558_toc">

3756Genotyping for founder pathogenic variants in <em class="gene-name">

3757BRCA1</em>

3758 and <em class="gene-name">

3759BRCA2</em>

3760</h4>

3761<p id="_1559" tabindex="-1">

3762In March 2018, the FDA authorized 23andMe to market DTC testing for three founder pathogenic variants in the <em class="gene-name">

3763BRCA1</em>

3764 and <em class="gene-name">

3765BRCA2</em>

3766 genes that are common in individuals of Ashkenazi Jewish descent.[<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

376756</a>

3768] These three variants are rare among high-risk individuals who are not of this ethnicity and in the general population of non-Jewish individuals. However, Jewish individuals whose family history is suggestive of hereditary breast/ovarian cancer who test negative for these three variants warrant additional testing.</p>

3769<p id="_1560" tabindex="-1">

3770It is crucial for individuals who obtain a <em class="gene-name">

3771BRCA1</em>

3772/<em class="gene-name">

3773BRCA2</em>

3774 (or any health-related) positive result from DTC testing to pursue clinical confirmation of such a result. Clinical confirmation entails repeating the test in a <a href="https://www.cms.gov/Regulations-and-Guidance/Legislation/CLIA/index?redirect=/CLIA/09_CLIA_Regulations_and_Federal_Register_Documents.asp#TopOfPage" title="https://www.cms.gov/Regulations-and-Guidance/Legislation/CLIA/index?redirect=/CLIA/09_CLIA_Regulations_and_Federal_Register_Documents.asp#TopOfPage">

3775CLIA-certified laboratory</a>

3776, as well as individual review and verification of the result by laboratory personnel.</p>

3777<p id="_1561" tabindex="-1">

3778A potential advantage of DTC testing of these three <em class="gene-name">

3779BRCA1</em>

3780/<em class="gene-name">

3781BRCA2</em>

3782 pathogenic variants is that it will identify individuals who would not have been otherwise aware of their increased risk of associated cancers, for example if they have no personal or family history of breast, ovarian, or prostate cancer. This is one of the main arguments for population-based screening for <em class="gene-name">

3783BRCA1</em>

3784/<em class="gene-name">

3785BRCA2</em>

3786 pathogenic variants. </p>

3787<p id="_1570" tabindex="-1">

3788However, a negative result does not rule out other hereditary factors or account for other clinical indicators, genetic and nongenetic, of increased cancer risk. Thus, for most individuals who test negative for the three <em class="gene-name">

3789BRCA1</em>

3790/<em class="gene-name">

3791BRCA2</em>

3792 variants, the results do not provide reassurance about their cancer risks. For high-risk individuals in particular (i.e., those with a history suggestive of hereditary breast/ovarian cancer) a negative result from this limited testing is incomplete, given that it does not assess the presence or absence of other pathogenic variants in <em class="gene-name">

3793BRCA1</em>

3794/<em class="gene-name">

3795BRCA2</em>

3796 or in many other cancer-associated genes.</p>

3797</section>

3798<section id="_1661">

3799<h4 id="_1661_toc">

3800Consumer-directed clinical testing</h4>

3801<p id="_1662" tabindex="-1">

3802Consumer-directed clinical testing is used to describe a hybrid approach to genetic testing, whereupon clinical&#8211;grade genetic testing can be initiated and selected by a consumer; however, ordering of the test by an authorized provider (e.g., primary care physician, nurse practitioner, or genetic counselor) is required.[<a href="#cit/section_6.57">

380357</a>

3804] The test ordering may be coordinated by the testing company. Genetic counseling may also be offered by the laboratory to explain the results. </p>

3805<p id="_1663" tabindex="-1">

3806With respect to cancer genetic testing, there are clinical, CLIA-certified laboratories that offer multigene (panel) tests as a consumer-directed service. Things to consider when genetic testing is ordered this way include: </p>

3807<div class="pdq-content-list">

3808<ol id="_1664">

3809<li>

3810<strong>

3811Is the test the same as what would have been ordered by the individual&#8217;s own health care provider after review of personal and family history?</strong>

3812<p id="_1665" tabindex="-1">

3813Multigene (panel) tests may not include all high- to moderate-risk genes in the differential, or newer/preliminary evidence genes.</p>

3814</li>

3815<li>

3816<strong>

3817Are variants of uncertain significance (VUS) reported back to the consumer, and if so, is the individual re-contacted if the VUS is reclassified?</strong>

3818<p id="_1666" tabindex="-1">

3819This is an important question to ask before testing is performed.</p>

3820</li>

3821<li>

3822<strong>

3823What is the cost of testing?</strong>

3824<p id="_1667" tabindex="-1">

3825Particularly for individuals who meet criteria for testing, insurance may cover the cost, whereas the consumer is responsible for the costs of consumer-directed testing. However, for individuals who do not meet criteria for testing and/or for whom insurance will not pay, the cost of consumer-directed testing may not be higher than out-of-pocket costs when ordered after pretest genetic counseling. </p>

3826<p id="_1668" tabindex="-1">

3827Some insurance companies require patients to have pretest genetic counseling by a credentialed genetics provider (and to meet specific eligibility criteria) in order for the testing to be covered. Consumer-directed testing thus eliminates the need for this requirement.</p>

3828</li>

3829</ol>

3830</div>

3831</section>

3832<section id="_1440">

3833<h4 id="_1440_toc">

3834Testing for single nucleotide variants (SNVs)</h4>

3835<p id="_1441" tabindex="-1">

3836In the past, several DTC companies offered only <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=803525&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000803525&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3837SNV</a>

3838-based testing to generate information about health risks, including risks of cancer. Selection of SNVs may be based on data from <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=636779&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000636779&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3839genome-wide association studies</a>

3840 (GWAS); however, there is no validated algorithm outlining how to generate cancer risk estimates from different SNVs, which individually are generally associated with modestly increased disease risks (usually conferring odds ratios &lt;2.0) or modestly decreased disease risks.[<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

384158</a>

3842] For more information, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/overview-pdq#_147">

3843GWAS</a>

3844 section in Cancer Genetics Overview. As a result, predicted disease risks from different DTC companies may yield different results. For example, a sample comparison of SNV-based risk prediction from two different companies for four different cancers yielded relative risks of 0.64 to 1.42 (excluding the three Ashkenazi <em class="gene-name">

3845BRCA1</em>

3846/<em class="gene-name">

3847BRCA2</em>

3848 founder pathogenic variants).[<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

384959</a>

3850] In addition, because commercial companies use different panels of SNVs, there is seldom concordance about the predicted risks for common diseases, and such risk estimates have not been prospectively validated.[<a href="#cit/section_6.60">

385160</a>

3852,<a href="#cit/section_6.61">

385361</a>

3854]</p>

3855<p id="_1442" tabindex="-1">

3856Another area of investigation is whether predicted disease risks from SNV testing are consistent with family history&#8211;based assessments. Studies using data from one commercial personal genomic testing company revealed that there was generally poor concordance between the SNV and family history risk assessment for common cancers such as breast, prostate, and colon.[<a href="#cit/section_6.62">

385762</a>

3858-<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

385964</a>

3860] Importantly, one of these studies highlighted that the majority of individuals with family histories suggestive of hereditary breast/ovarian cancer or Lynch syndrome received SNV results yielding lifetime cancer risks that were average or below average.[<a href="#cit/section_6.62">

386162</a>

3862]</p>

3863<p id="_1443" tabindex="-1">

3864Studies have begun to examine whether SNV testing could be used together with other established risk factors to assess the likelihood of developing cancer. For example, adding SNV data to validated breast cancer prediction tools such as those included in the <a href="https://bcrisktool.cancer.gov/" title="https://bcrisktool.cancer.gov/">

3865National Cancer Institute's Breast Cancer Risk Assessment Tool</a>

3866 (based on the Gail model) [<a href="#cit/section_6.65">

386765</a>

3868] may improve the accuracy of risk assessment.[<a href="#cit/section_6.66">

386966</a>

3870,<a href="#cit/section_6.67">

387167</a>

3872] However, this approach is not currently FDA-approved.</p>

3873<p id="_1444" tabindex="-1">

3874These findings underscore that SNV testing has not been validated as an accurate risk assessment tool and does not replace the collection, integration, and interpretation of personal and family history risk factor information by qualified health care professionals.</p>

3875</section>

3876<section id="_1445">

3877<h4 id="_1445_toc">

3878DTC whole-exome/genome sequencing and interpretation</h4>

3879<p id="_1446" tabindex="-1">

3880Increasingly, DTC testing companies offer <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=740456&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000740456&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3881whole-genome sequencing</a>

3882 (WGS) or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=740459&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000740459&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3883whole-exome sequencing</a>

3884 (WES), including SNV data. For more information on WGS and WES, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/overview-pdq#_2594">

3885Clinical Sequencing</a>

3886 section in Cancer Genetics Overview. In addition, consumers who submit their DNA to a DTC lab may have access to their raw sequence data and may consult with other companies, websites, and open-access databases for interpretation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

388768</a>

3888,<a href="#cit/section_6.69">

388969</a>

3890] However, these data must be interpreted with caution. A clinical lab found that 40% of variants reported in DTC raw data were false positives (i.e., low analytic validity because the identified variant was not present).[<a href="#cit/section_6.70">

389170</a>

3892] In addition, several variants that were designated as &#8220;increased risk&#8221; in the raw data were classified as benign by clinical laboratories and public databases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.70">

389370</a>

3894] Given the potential for misinterpretation, which may lead to unnecessary medical procedures or testing, these findings underscore the importance of clinical confirmation of all potentially medically actionable gene variants identified by DTC testing.</p>

3895<p id="_1447" tabindex="-1">

3896Some factors to consider when determining the accuracy and utility of sequence data for cancer (or other disease) risk assessment include the <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=778722&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000778722&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3897sequencing depth</a>

3898 of the genes of interest, whether large rearrangements or gene deletions would be detected, and whether or how positive results are confirmed (e.g., through Sanger sequencing). For example, if sequencing depth is low or rare variants cannot be detected, then there is a concern about false-negative results. There is also a risk that sequence changes will be erroneously labeled as pathogenic when confirmatory testing or different interpretative approaches would determine that the variant identified is benign (false positive). When WES or WGS is performed, VUS are also likely to be identified,[<a href="#cit/section_6.71">

389971</a>

3900] and DTC companies have varying protocols for classification, which may or may not be consistent with national guidelines.[<a href="#cit/section_6.72">

390172</a>

3902] In addition, as evidence evolves and variants are reclassified, consumers need to be aware of the process the DTC lab has, if any, for updating information and re-contacting consumers with revised interpretations.</p>

3903</section>

3904<section id="_1448">

3905<h4 id="_1448_toc">

3906Considerations</h4>

3907<p id="_1449" tabindex="-1">

3908There may be potential benefits associated with DTC testing. DTC marketing and provision of genetic tests may promote patient autonomy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

390959</a>

3910] Individuals may develop an increased awareness of the importance of family history, the relationship between risk and family history, the role of genetics in disease, and a better understanding of the value of genetic counseling.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

391173</a>

3912] Although results of SNV-based DTC testing appear to motivate some individuals to seek the advice of their doctor, make lifestyle changes, and pursue screening tests,[<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

391374</a>

3914-<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

391577</a>

3916] short-term modest effects on risk perception after notification of an elevated risk (e.g., for cancer) may not significantly alter lifestyle or cancer screening behaviors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.78">

391778</a>

3918,<a href="#cit/section_6.79">

391979</a>

3920] Further, psychological distress has not been widely reported among consumers who have undergone DTC testing for a variety of conditions.[<a href="#cit/section_6.76">

392176</a>

3922] However, little is known about how individuals respond after learning that they carry pathogenic variants in high-risk genes such as <em class="gene-name">

3923BRCA1</em>

3924/<em class="gene-name">

3925BRCA2</em>

3926 when testing is performed within a DTC context and without traditional forms of pre- and posttest genetic education and counseling.</p>

3927<p id="_1450" tabindex="-1">

3928Given the complexity of genomic testing, several professional organizations have released position statements about DTC genetic testing. For example, in 2010, ASCO published a position statement outlining several considerations related to DTC cancer genomic tests, including those mentioned above.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

39291</a>

3930] They endorsed pre- and posttest genetic counseling and informed consent by qualified health care professionals. ASCO&#8217;s 2015 position statement on genetic and genomic testing for cancer susceptibility reinforces the importance of provider education given the complexity of genomic testing and interpretation and discusses their recommendations for regulatory review of genomic tests, including those offered by DTC companies.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

39312</a>

3932]</p>

3933<p id="_1451" tabindex="-1">

3934In 2016, a statement by the American College of Medical Genetics and Genomics about DTC genetic testing similarly endorsed the involvement of qualified genetics professionals in the processes of test ordering and interpretation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.80">

393580</a>

3936] The statement also emphasized the need to incorporate established methods of risk assessment into disease risk prediction (such as personal and family medical history information) and stressed that consumers need to be informed about the potential limitations and risks associated with DTC testing. </p>

3937</section>

3938</section>

3939<section id="_226">

3940<h3 id="_226_toc">

3941Informed Consent</h3>

3942<p id="_1195" tabindex="-1">

3943<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

3944Informed consent</a>

3945 can enhance preparedness for testing, including careful weighing of benefits and limitations of testing, minimization of adverse psychosocial outcomes, appropriate use of medical options, and a strengthened provider-patient relationship based on honesty, support, and trust.</p>

3946<p id="_227" tabindex="-1">

3947Consensus exists among experts that a process of informed consent should be

3948an integral part of the pretest counseling process.[<a href="#cit/section_6.81">

394981</a>

3950] This view is driven

3951by several ethical dilemmas that can arise in genetic susceptibility testing. The most commonly cited concern is the

3952possibility of insurance or employment discrimination if a test result, or even

3953the fact that an individual has sought or is seeking testing, is disclosed. In 2008, Congress passed the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1792">

3954Genetic Information Nondiscrimination Act of 2008 (GINA)</a>

3955. This federal law provides protections related to health insurance and employment discrimination based on genetic information. However, GINA does not cover life, disability, or long-term-care insurance discrimination.[<a href="#cit/section_6.82">

395682</a>

3957] For more information, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1792">

3958GINA</a>

3959 section. A related issue involves

3960stigmatization that may occur when an individual who may never develop the

3961condition in question, or may not do so for decades, receives genetic

3962information and is labeled or labels himself or herself as ill. Finally, in the case

3963of genetic testing, medical information given to one individual

3964has immediate implications for biologic relatives. These implications include

3965not only the medical risks but also disruptions in familial relationships. The

3966possibility for coercion exists when one family member wants to be tested but,

3967to do so optimally, must first obtain genetic material or information from

3968other family members.</p>

3969<p id="_228" tabindex="-1">

3970Inclusion of an informed consent process in counseling can facilitate patient autonomy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.83">

397183</a>

3972] It may also reduce the potential for misunderstanding

3973between patient and provider. Many clinical programs provide opportunities for

3974individuals to review their informed consent during the genetic testing and

3975counseling process. Initial informed consent provides a

3976verbal and/or written overview of the process. </p>

3977<p id="_229" tabindex="-1">

3978Some programs use a second informed consent process prior to disclosure to the

3979individual of his or her genetic test results. This process allows for the possibility

3980that a person may change his or her mind about receiving test results. After

3981the test result has been disclosed, a third informed consent discussion often

3982occurs. This discussion concerns issues regarding sharing the genetic test result with

3983health providers and/or interested family members, currently or in the future.

3984Obtaining written permission to provide the test result to others in the family who are at risk can avoid vexing problems in the future should the individual not be

3985available to release his or her results.</p>

3986<section id="_231">

3987<h4 id="_231_toc">

3988Core elements of informed consent</h4>

3989<p id="_232" tabindex="-1">

3990Major elements of an informed consent discussion are highlighted in the

3991preceding discussion. The critical elements, as described in the literature,[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

39921</a>

3993,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

39942</a>

3995,<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

399684</a>

3997,<a href="#cit/section_6.85">

399885</a>

3999] include the following:

4000</p>

4001<div class="pdq-content-list">

4002<ul id="_94">

4003<li>

4004Specific test being performed.</li>

4005<li>

4006Elicitation and discussion of a person&#8217;s expectations, beliefs, goals, and

4007motivations.

4008</li>

4009<li>

4010Explanation of how inheritance of genetic factors may affect cancer

4011susceptibility.

4012</li>

4013<li>

4014Clarification of a person&#8217;s increased risk status.

4015</li>

4016<li>

4017Discussion of potential benefits, risks, and limitations of testing.

4018</li>

4019<li>

4020Discussion of costs and logistics of testing and follow-up.

4021</li>

4022<li>

4023Discussion of possible outcomes of testing (e.g., true positive, true negative,

4024VUS, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=556498&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000556498&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4025inconclusive</a>

4026, false positive, false negative, secondary findings).

4027</li>

4028<li>

4029Discussion of medical management options based on risk assessment and/or test results available for those who choose to test, for those who choose not to test, and for those who have positive, negative, or inconclusive results.

4030</li>

4031<li>

4032Data on efficacy of methods of cancer prevention and early detection.

4033</li>

4034<li>

4035Discussion of possible psychological, social, economic, and family

4036dynamic ramifications of testing or not testing.

4037</li>

4038<li>

4039Discussion of a genetic test result's implications for family members; patients may be asked to inform their family member(s) about their genetic test results.[<a href="#cit/section_6.86">

404086</a>

4041]</li>

4042<li>

4043Discussion of alternatives to genetic testing (e.g., tissue banking, risk

4044assessment without genetic testing).

4045</li>

4046<li>

4047Attainment of verbal and written informed consent or clarification of the decision to

4048decline testing.

4049</li>

4050</ul>

4051</div>

4052<p id="_95" tabindex="-1">

4053All individuals considering genetic testing should be informed that they have

4054several options even after the genetic testing has been completed. They may

4055decide to receive the results at the posttest meeting, delay result

4056notification, or less commonly, not receive the results of testing. They should be

4057informed that their interest in receiving results will be addressed at the

4058beginning of the posttest meeting and that time will be available to

4059review their concerns and thoughts on notification. It is important that

4060individuals receive this information during the pretest counseling to ensure

4061added comfort with the decision to decline or defer result notification even

4062when test results become available.

4063</p>

4064</section>

4065<section id="_233">

4066<h4 id="_233_toc">

4067Testing in children</h4>

4068<p id="_234" tabindex="-1">

4069Genetic testing for pathogenic variants in cancer susceptibility genes in children is particularly complex. While both parents [<a href="#cit/section_6.87">

407087</a>

4071] and providers [<a href="#cit/section_6.88">

407288</a>

4073] may request or recommend testing for minor children, many experts recommend that unless there is evidence that the test result will influence the medical management of the child or adolescent, genetic testing should be deferred until legal adulthood (age 18 y or older) because of concerns about autonomy, potential discrimination, and possible psychosocial effects.[<a href="#cit/section_6.89">

407489</a>

4075-<a href="#cit/section_6.91">

407691</a>

4077] A number of cancer syndromes include childhood disease risk, such as retinoblastoma, multiple endocrine neoplasia (MEN) types 1 and 2 (MEN1 and MEN2), neurofibromatosis types 1 and 2 (NF1 and NF2), Beckwith&#8211;Wiedemann syndrome, Fanconi anemia, FAP, and Von Hippel-Lindau disease (VHL).[<a href="#cit/section_6.92">

407892</a>

4079,<a href="#cit/section_6.93">

408093</a>

4081] As a consequence, decisions about genetic testing in children are made in the context of a specific gene in which a pathogenic variant is suspected. The ASCO statement on genetic testing for cancer susceptibility maintains that the decision to consider offering childhood genetic testing should take into account not only the risk of childhood malignancy but also the evidence associated with risk reduction interventions for that disorder.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

40821</a>

4083] Specifically, ASCO recommends that:</p>

4084<div class="pdq-content-list">

4085<ul id="_300">

4086<li>

4087When screening or preventive strategies during childhood are available (e.g., MEN and FAP), testing should be encouraged on clinical grounds.</li>

4088<li>

4089When no risk reduction strategies are available in childhood and the probability of developing a malignancy during childhood is very low (e.g., hereditary breast/ovarian cancer syndrome), testing should not be offered.</li>

4090<li>

4091Some patients may be at risk of developing a malignancy during childhood without the availability of validated risk-reduction strategies (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

4092TP53</em>

4093 pathogenic variants). The decision to test in such circumstances is particularly controversial.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

40941</a>

4095]</li>

4096</ul>

4097</div>

4098<p id="_301" tabindex="-1">

4099Special considerations are required when genetic counseling and testing for pathogenic variants in cancer susceptibility genes are considered in children. The first issue is the age of the child. Young children, especially those younger than 10 years, may not be involved or may have limited involvement in the decision to be tested, and some may not participate in the genetic counseling process. In these cases, the child&#8217;s parents or other legal surrogate will be involved in the genetic counseling and will ultimately be responsible for making the decision to proceed with testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

41001</a>

4101,<a href="#cit/section_6.94">

410294</a>

4103] Counseling under these circumstances incorporates a discussion of how test results will be shared with the child when he or she is older.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

41041</a>

4105] Children aged 10 to 17 years may have more involvement in the decision-making process.[<a href="#cit/section_6.95">

410695</a>

4107] In a qualitative study of parents and children aged 10 to 17 years assessing decision making for genetic research participation, older, more mature children and families with open communication styles were more likely to have joint decision making. The majority of children in this study felt that they should have the right to make the final decision for genetic research participation, although many would seek input from their parents.[<a href="#cit/section_6.95">

410895</a>

4109] While this study is specific to genetic research participation, the findings allude to the importance children aged 10 to 17 years place on personal decision making regarding factors that impact them. Unfortunately cognitive and psychosocial development may not consistently correlate with the age of the child.[<a href="#cit/section_6.94">

411094</a>

4111] Therefore, careful assessment of the child&#8217;s developmental stage may help in the genetic counseling and testing process to facilitate parent and child adaptation to the test results. Another complicating factor includes potential risks for discrimination. For more information, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1787">

4112Employment and Insurance Discrimination</a>

4113 section.</p>

4114<p id="_302" tabindex="-1">

4115The consequences of genetic testing in children have been reviewed.[<a href="#cit/section_6.94">

411694</a>

4117] In contrast to observations in adults, young children in particular are vulnerable to changes in parent and child bonding based on test results. Genetic testing could interfere with the development of self-concept and self-esteem. Children may also be at risk of developing feelings of survivor guilt or heightened anxiety. All children are especially susceptible to not understanding the testing, results, or implications for their health. As children mature, they begin to have decreased dependency on their parents while developing their personal identity. This can be altered in the setting of a serious health condition or an inherited disorder. Older children are beginning to mature physically and develop intimate relationships while also changing their idealized view of their parents. All of this can be influenced by the results of a genetic test.[<a href="#cit/section_6.94">

411894</a>

4119] In its recommendations for genetic testing in asymptomatic minors, the European Society of Human Genetics emphasizes that parents have a responsibility to inform their children about their genetic risk and to communicate this information in a way that is tailored to the child&#8217;s age and developmental level.[<a href="#cit/section_6.96">

412096</a>

4121,<a href="#cit/section_6.97">

412297</a>

4123]</p>

4124<p id="_303" tabindex="-1">

4125 In summary, genetic testing may be considered in children if the results may alter the child's medical treatment and positively impact his/her health outcomes. Deferral of genetic testing is suggested when the risk of childhood malignancy is low or absent and/or there is no evidence that interventions can reduce risk.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

41261</a>

4127] When genetic testing is offered to a child, the child&#8217;s developmental stage is used to determine his/her involvement and who has legal authority to provide consent. It is important for providers to understand a family's psychosocial state and dynamics regarding potential genetic test results; this will enable the provider to give appropriate support when disclosing the child's genetic test result.</p>

4128<p id="_1921" tabindex="-1">

4129For more information about testing children for hereditary cancer syndromes, see the following PDQ summaries:</p>

4130<div class="pdq-content-list">

4131<ul id="_1920">

4132<li>

4133<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_972">

4134Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers</a>

4135</li>

4136<li>

4137<a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_189">

4138Genetics of Colorectal Cancer</a>

4139</li>

4140<li>

4141<a href="/types/thyroid/hp/medullary-thyroid-genetics-pdq#_126">

4142Genetics of Endocrine and Neuroendocrine Neoplasias</a>

4143</li>

4144</ul>

4145</div>

4146</section>

4147<section id="_235">

4148<h4 id="_235_toc">

4149Testing in vulnerable populations</h4>

4150<p id="_236" tabindex="-1">

4151Genetic counseling and testing require special considerations when used in vulnerable populations. In 1995, the American Society of Human Genetics published a position statement on the ethical, legal, and psychosocial implications of genetic testing in children and adolescents as a vulnerable population.[<a href="#cit/section_6.90">

415290</a>

4153] However, vulnerable populations encompass more than just children. Federal policy applicable to research involving human subjects, <a href="https://www.hhs.gov/ohrp/regulations-and-policy/regulations/45-cfr-46/index.html" title="https://www.hhs.gov/ohrp/regulations-and-policy/regulations/45-cfr-46/index.html">

415445 CFR Code of Federal Regulations part 46 Protection Of Human Subjects</a>

4155, considers the following groups as potentially vulnerable populations: prisoners, traumatized and comatose patients, terminally ill patients, elderly/aged individuals who are cognitively impaired and/or institutionalized, minorities, students, employees, and individuals from outside the United States. Specific to genetic testing, the <a href="https://www.isong.org/resources/Documents/PS_Genetic_Counseling.pdf" title="https://www.isong.org/resources/Documents/PS_Genetic_Counseling.pdf">

4156International Society of Nurses in Genetics</a>

4157 further expanded the definition of vulnerable populations to also include individuals with hearing and language deficits or conditions limiting communication (for example, language differences and concerns with reliable translation), cognitive impairment, psychiatric disturbances, clients undergoing stress due to a family situation, those without financial resources, clients with acute or chronic illness and in end-of-life, and those in whom medication may impair reasoning. </p>

4158<p id="_299" tabindex="-1">

4159Genetic counseling and testing in vulnerable populations raises special considerations. The aim of genetic counseling is to help people understand and adapt to the medical, psychological, and familial implications of genetic contributions to disease, which in part involves the meaningful exchange of factual information.[<a href="#cit/section_6.98">

416098</a>

4161] In a vulnerable population, health care providers need to be sensitive to factors that can impact the ability of the individual to comprehend the information. In particular, in circumstances of cognitive impairment or intellectual disability, special attention is paid to whether the individual&#8217;s legally authorized representative should be involved in the counseling, informed consent, and testing process. </p>

4162<p id="_351" tabindex="-1">

4163Providers need to assess all patients for their ability to make an uncoerced, autonomous, informed decision prior to proceeding with genetic testing. Populations that do not seem vulnerable (e.g., legally adult college students) may actually be deemed vulnerable because of undue coercion for testing by their parents or the threat of withholding financial support by their parents based on a testing decision inconsistent with the parent&#8217;s wishes. Alteration of the genetic counseling and testing process may be necessary depending on the situation, such as counseling and testing in terminally ill individuals who opt for testing for the benefit of their children, but given their impending death, results may have no impact on their own health care or may not be available before their death. In summary, genetic counseling and testing requires that the health care provider assess all individuals for any evidence of vulnerability, and if present, be sensitive to those issues, modify genetic counseling based on the specific circumstances, and avoid causing additional harm.</p>

4164</section>

4165</section>

4166<section id="_96">

4167<h3 id="_96_toc">

4168Importance of Pretest Counseling</h3>

4169<p id="_97" tabindex="-1">

4170The complexity of genetic testing for cancer susceptibility has led experts to

4171suggest that careful, in-depth counseling should precede any decision about the

4172use of testing, in keeping with the accepted principles for the use of genetic

4173testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.99">

417499</a>

4175] </p>

4176<p id="_98" tabindex="-1">

4177Qualitative and quantitative research studies indicate that families hold a

4178variety of beliefs about the inheritance of characteristics within families;

4179some of these beliefs are congruent with current scientific understanding, whereas

4180others are not.[<a href="#cit/section_6.100">

4181100</a>

4182-<a href="#cit/section_6.102">

4183102</a>

4184] These beliefs may be influenced by education, personal

4185and family experiences, and cultural background. Because behavior is

4186likely to be influenced by these beliefs, the usefulness of genetic information

4187may depend on recognizing and addressing the individual&#8217;s preexisting

4188cognitions. This process begins with initial discussion and continues

4189throughout the genetic counseling process.

4190</p>

4191</section>

4192<section id="_99">

4193<h3 id="_99_toc">

4194Psychological Impact of Genetic Information/Test Results on the Individual

4195</h3>

4196<p id="_100" tabindex="-1">

4197An accurate assessment of psychosocial functioning and emotional factors

4198related to testing motivation and potential impact and utilization is an

4199important part of pretest counseling.[<a href="#cit/section_6.103">

4200103</a>

4201-<a href="#cit/section_6.107">

4202107</a>

4203] Generally, a provider inquires

4204about a person&#8217;s emotional response to the family history of cancer and also

4205about a person&#8217;s response to his or her own risk of developing cancer. People

4206have various coping strategies for dealing with stressful circumstances such as

4207genetic risk. Identifying these strategies and ascertaining how well or poorly

4208they work will have implications for the support necessary during posttest

4209counseling and will help personalize the discussion of anticipated risks and

4210benefits of testing. Taking a brief history of past and current psychiatric

4211symptoms (e.g., depression, extreme anxiety, or suicidality) will allow for an

4212assessment of whether this individual is at particular risk of adverse

4213effects after disclosure of results. In such cases, further psychological

4214assessment may be indicated.

4215</p>

4216<p id="_101" tabindex="-1">

4217In addition, cognitive deficits in the person being counseled may significantly limit

4218understanding of the genetic information provided and hinder the ability to

4219give informed consent and may also require further psychological assessment.

4220Emotional responses to cancer risk may also affect overall mood and functioning

4221in other areas of life such as home, work, and personal health management, including cancer screening practices.[<a href="#cit/section_6.108">

4222108</a>

4223] Education and genetic counseling

4224sessions provide an ongoing opportunity for informal assessment of affective and cognitive aspects of the communication process.

4225Since behavioral factors influence adherence to screening and surveillance

4226recommendations, consideration of emotional barriers is important in helping a

4227person choose prevention strategies and in discussing the potential

4228utility of genetic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_6.109">

4229109</a>

4230,<a href="#cit/section_6.110">

4231110</a>

4232]</p>

4233<p id="_102" tabindex="-1">

4234The discussion of issues such as history of depression, anxiety, and suicidal

4235thoughts or tendencies requires sensitivity to the individual. The individual

4236must be assured that the counseling process is a collaborative effort to

4237minimize intrusiveness while maximizing benefits. Determining whether the individual is currently receiving treatment for major psychiatric illness is an important part of the counseling process. Consultation with a mental health professional familiar

4238with psychological assessments may be useful to help the provider develop

4239the strategies for these discussions. It also may be beneficial for the individual to

4240be given standard psychological self-report instruments that assess levels of

4241depression, anxiety, and other psychiatric difficulties that he or she may be

4242experiencing. This step provides objective comparisons with already established

4243normative data.[<a href="#cit/section_6.111">

4244111</a>

4245,<a href="#cit/section_6.112">

4246112</a>

4247]</p>

4248<p id="_103" tabindex="-1">

4249In addition to the clinical assessment of psychological functioning, several instruments for cancer patients and people at increased risk of cancer

4250have been utilized to assess psychological

4251status. These include the Center for Epidemiological Studies-Depression

4252 scale,[<a href="#cit/section_6.113">

4253113</a>

4254] the Profile of Mood States,[<a href="#cit/section_6.114">

4255114</a>

4256] the Hospital Anxiety and

4257Depression Scale,[<a href="#cit/section_6.115">

4258115</a>

4259] and the Brief Symptom Inventory.[<a href="#cit/section_6.116">

4260116</a>

4261] Research programs have included one or more of these

4262instruments as a way of helping refine the selection of people at increased

4263risk of adverse psychosocial consequences of genetic testing. Psychological

4264assessments are an ongoing part of genetic counseling. Some individuals

4265with symptoms of increased distress, extreme avoidance of affect, or other marked

4266psychiatric symptoms may benefit from a discussion with, or evaluation by, a mental

4267health professional. It may be suggested to some people (generally, a very

4268small percentage of any population) that testing be postponed until greater

4269emotional stability has been established.

4270</p>

4271</section>

4272<section id="_104">

4273<h3 id="_104_toc">

4274Psychological Impact of Genetic Information/Test Results on the Family</h3>

4275<p id="_105" tabindex="-1">

4276In addition to assessing the family history of cancer, the family

4277as a social system may also be assessed as part of the process of cancer

4278genetic counseling. Hereditary susceptibility to cancer may affect social

4279interactions and attitudes toward the family.[<a href="#cit/section_6.117">

4280117</a>

4281]

4282</p>

4283<p id="_106" tabindex="-1">

4284In assessing families, characteristics that may be relevant are the

4285organization of the family (including recognition of individuals who propose to

4286speak for or motivate other family members), patterns of communication within

4287the family, cohesion or closeness of family members (or lack thereof), and the

4288family beliefs and values that affect health behaviors. Ethnocultural factors may

4289also play an important role in guiding behavior in some families.

4290</p>

4291<p id="_107" tabindex="-1">

4292Assessment also evaluates the impact of the family&#8217;s prior experience

4293with illness on their attitudes and behaviors related to genetic counseling and

4294testing. Prior experience with cancer diagnosis and treatment, loss due to

4295cancer, and the family members&#8217; interaction with the medical community may

4296heavily influence attitudes toward receiving genetic information and may play a

4297major role in the emotional state of individuals presenting for genetic

4298services.

4299</p>

4300<p id="_108" tabindex="-1">

4301The practitioner may use the above framework to guide inquiries about the

4302relationship of the individual to (1) the affected members of the family or (2)

4303others who are considering or deciding against the consideration of genetic

4304counseling or testing. Inquiries about how the family shares (or does not

4305share) information about health, illness, and genetic susceptibility may

4306establish whether the individual feels under pressure from other family members

4307or anticipates difficulty in sharing genetic information obtained from

4308counseling or testing. Inquiries about the present health (new diagnoses or

4309deaths from cancer) or relationship status (divorce, marriage, grieving) of

4310family members may inform the provider about the timing of the individual&#8217;s

4311participation in counseling or testing and may also reveal possible

4312contraindications for testing at present.

4313</p>

4314<p id="_1257" tabindex="-1">

4315Evidence from a study of 297 individuals from 38 Lynch syndrome&#8211;affected families suggested that the timing of genetic counseling and testing services may influence psychological test-related distress responses. Specifically, family members in the same generation as the proband were more likely to experience greater test-related distress with increasingly longer lengths of time between the proband's receipt of MMR pathogenic variant results and the provision of genetic counseling and testing services to family members. However, it was unclear whether time lapses were due to a delay in the proband communicating test results or the family member choosing to delay genetic testing, despite being aware of the proband's results.[<a href="#cit/section_6.118">

4316118</a>

4317] For more information on how families cope with hereditary cancer syndromes, see the psychosocial/counseling sections in <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_271">

4318Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers</a>

4319 and <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-genetics-pdq#_189">

4320Genetics of Colorectal Cancer</a>

4321.</p>

4322</section>
