4783 <section id="_1608">

4784

4785 <h2>

4786Risk Communication</h2>

4787

4788 <div id="_section_1608" class="pdq-sections">

4789<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4790<h6>

4791In This Section</h6>

4792<ul>

4793<li>

4794<a href="#_354_toc">

4795Methods of Risk Presentation</a>

4796</li>

4797<li>

4798<a href="#_218_toc">

4799Communication Strategies</a>

4800</li>

4801<li>

4802<a href="#_122_toc">

4803Posttest Education and Result Notification</a>

4804</li>

4805<li>

4806<a href="#_1609_toc">

4807Cascade Genetic Testing of Family Members<ul>

4808<li>

4809<a href="#_1676_toc">

4810Uptake of cascade genetic testing</a>

4811</li>

4812<li>

4813<a href="#_1684_toc">

4814Strategies to facilitate cascade genetic testing</a>

4815</li>

4816<li>

4817<a href="#_1704_toc">

4818Ethical, legal, and social issues</a>

4819</li>

4820</ul>

4821</a>

4822</li>

4823</ul>

4824</nav>

4825<p id="_75" tabindex="-1">

4826Specific clinical programs for risk management may be offered to individuals with

4827an increased genetic risk of cancer. These programs may differ from those

4828offered to individuals of average risk in several ways: <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000046171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4829screening</a>

4830 may be initiated

4831at an earlier age or involve shorter screening intervals; screening strategies

4832not in routine use, such as screening for ovarian cancer, may be offered; and

4833interventions to reduce cancer risk, such as risk-reducing surgery, may be

4834offered. Current

4835recommendations are summarized in the PDQ summaries addressing the genetics of

4836specific cancers.

4837</p>

4838<p id="_1080" tabindex="-1">

4839The goal of genetic education and <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4840counseling</a>

4841 is to help individuals understand their personal risk status, recognize their options for cancer risk management, and explore their feelings regarding their personal risk status. Counseling focuses on obtaining and giving information, promoting autonomous decision making, and facilitating <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4842informed consent</a>

4843 if genetic testing is pursued.</p>

4844<p id="_76" tabindex="-1">

4845Optimally, education and counseling about <strong>

4846cancer risk</strong>

4847 includes providing the following information:</p>

4848<div class="pdq-content-list">

4849<ul id="_1081">

4850<li>

4851Purpose, strengths, and limitations of cancer <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460214&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460214&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4852risk assessment</a>

4853.</li>

4854<li>

4855Basic genetics and patterns of inheritance.</li>

4856<li>

4857Genetic basis of cancer.</li>

4858<li>

4859Clinical features of relevant hereditary cancer syndromes.</li>

4860<li>

4861Evidence of a hereditary cancer syndrome from the <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460140&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460140&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4862consultand's</a>

4863 personal and family history.</li>

4864<li>

4865Options for clarifying cancer risk, including genetic testing, if indicated.</li>

4866<li>

4867Options available for risk management, including data (or lack of data) on the efficacy of different measures for early detection and risk reduction.</li>

4868<li>

4869Signs and symptoms of cancer.</li>

4870</ul>

4871</div>

4872<p id="_1083" tabindex="-1">

4873When a clinically valid genetic test is available, education and counseling for <strong>

4874genetic testing</strong>

4875 typically includes the following:</p>

4876<div class="pdq-content-list">

4877<ul id="_1084">

4878<li>

4879Risk of having a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4880pathogenic variant</a>

4881 and patterns of transmission.</li>

4882<li>

4883Alternatives to genetic testing.</li>

4884<li>

4885Risks, benefits, and limitations of genetic testing, including psychological and discriminatory risks.</li>

4886<li>

4887Possible test outcomes, including likelihood of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=556485&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000556485&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4888uninformative results</a>

4889 and identifying <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=556495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000556495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4890variants of uncertain significance</a>

4891.</li>

4892<li>

4893Sensitivity of the genetic test, including the techniques utilized to perform the test and their associated limitations.</li>

4894<li>

4895Health care management options based on possible test results.</li>

4896<li>

4897Implications for children and other family members based on pattern of transmission.</li>

4898<li>

4899Dissemination of risk and genetic information to family members.</li>

4900<li>

4901Cost associated with testing, counseling, medical management, and options for insurance coverage.</li>

4902<li>

4903How genetic information and genetic test results will be recorded in the medical record.</li>

4904<li>

4905Specimen storage and reuse, if applicable.</li>

4906</ul>

4907</div>

4908<p id="_1085" tabindex="-1">

4909If a second session is held to <strong>

4910disclose and interpret genetic test results</strong>

4911, education and counseling focuses on the following:</p>

4912<div class="pdq-content-list">

4913<ul id="_1086">

4914<li>

4915Interpretation of test results.</li>

4916<li>

4917Discussion of further testing that may clarify risk (e.g., large rearrangement testing and testing the other genes based on the patient's differential cancer syndrome list).</li>

4918<li>

4919Assessment of the emotional and behavioral responses to genetic test results.</li>

4920<li>

4921Recommendations for coping and communication strategies to address issues related to cancer risk.</li>

4922<li>

4923Cancer risk management recommendations.</li>

4924<li>

4925Risk analysis and dissemination of risk results to family members.</li>

4926</ul>

4927</div>

4928<p id="_1087" tabindex="-1">

4929The process of counseling may require more than one visit to address medical, genetic testing, and psychosocial support issues. Additional case-related preparation time is spent before and after the consultation sessions to obtain and review medical records, complete case documentation, seek information about differential diagnoses, identify appropriate laboratories for genetic tests, find patient support groups, research resources, and communicate with or refer to other specialists.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

49301</a>

4931]</p>

4932<p id="_1088" tabindex="-1">

4933Information about inherited risk of cancer is growing rapidly. Many of the issues discussed in a counseling session may need to be revisited as new information emerges. At the end of the counseling process, individuals are typically reminded of the possibility that future research may provide new options and/or new information on risk. Individuals may be advised to check in with the health care provider periodically to determine whether new information is sufficient to merit an additional counseling session. The obligation of health care providers to recontact individuals when new genetic testing or treatment options are available is controversial, and standards have not been established.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

49342</a>

4935,<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

49363</a>

4937]</p>

4938<section id="_354">

4939<h3 id="_354_toc">

4940Methods of Risk Presentation</h3>

4941<p id="_355" tabindex="-1">

4942Using probability to communicate risk may overestimate risk certainty; this is especially true when risk estimates have wide confidence intervals or when the patient has characteristics that differ greatly from that of the sample that the risk estimate was based on. Finally, there are wide variations in individuals&#8217; level of understanding of mathematical concepts (i.e., numeracy). For all the above reasons, conveying risk in multiple ways, both numerically and verbally, with discussion of important caveats, may be a useful strategy to increase risk comprehension. The numerical format that facilitates the best understanding is natural frequencies because frequencies include information concerning the denominator, the reference group to which the individual may refer. In general, logarithmic scales are to be avoided.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

49434</a>

4944] Additionally, important &#8220;contextual&#8221; risks may be included with the frequency in order to increase risk comprehension; these may include how the person&#8217;s risk compares with those who do not have the risk factor in question and the risks associated with common hazards, such as being in a car accident. Additional suggestions include being consistent in risk formats (do not mix odds and percentages), using the same denominator across risk estimates, avoiding decimal points, including base rate information, and providing more explanation if the risk is less than 1%.</p>

4945<p id="_356" tabindex="-1">

4946The communication of risk may be numerical or visual. Use of multiple strategies may increase comprehension and retention of cancer genetic risk information.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

49474</a>

4948] Recently, use of visual risk communication strategies has increased (e.g., histograms, pie charts, and Venn diagrams). Visual depictions of risk may be very useful when working with visual learners, but research that confirms this is lacking.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

49495</a>

4950,<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

49516</a>

4952] A study published in 2008 examined the use of two different visual aids to communicate breast cancer risk. Women at an increased risk of breast cancer were randomized to receive feedback via a bar graph alone or a bar graph plus a frequency diagram (i.e., highlighted human figures). Overall results indicated that there were no differences in improved accuracy of risk perception between the two groups. However, there was a greater improvement in accuracy of risk perception among the group of women who inaccurately perceived very high risk at baseline and received both visual aids.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

49537</a>

4954]</p>

4955</section>

4956<section id="_218">

4957<h3 id="_218_toc">

4958Communication Strategies</h3>

4959<p id="_357" tabindex="-1">

4960Studies have examined novel channels to communicate genetic cancer risk information, deliver psychosocial support, and standardize the genetic counseling process for individuals at increased risk of cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

49618</a>

4962-<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

496315</a>

4964] Much of this literature has attempted to make the genetic counseling session more efficient or to limit the need for the counselor to address basic genetic principles in the session to free up time for the client&#8217;s personal and emotional concerns about his or her risk. For example, the receipt of genetic feedback for <em class="gene-name">

4965BRCA1</em>

4966/<em class="gene-name">

4967BRCA2</em>

4968 and mismatch repair gene testing by letter, rather than a face-to-face genetic counseling feedback session, has been investigated.[<a href="#cit/section_7.16">

496916</a>

4970] Other modalities include the development of patient assessments or checklists, CD-ROM programs, and interactive computer programs. </p>

4971<p id="_1248" tabindex="-1">

4972A prospective study evaluated the effects of a CD-ROM decisional support aid for <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285933&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000285933&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

4973microsatellite instability</a>

4974 (MSI) tumor testing in 239 colorectal cancer patients who met the revised Bethesda criteria but who did not meet the Amsterdam criteria.[<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

497517</a>

4976] The study also tested a theoretical model of factors influencing decisional conflict surrounding decisions to pursue MSI tumor testing. Within the study, half of the sample was randomly assigned to receive a brief description of MSI testing within the clinical encounter, and the other half was provided the CD-ROM decisional support aid in addition to the brief description. The CD-ROM and brief description intervention increased knowledge about MSI testing more than the brief description alone did. As a result, participants felt more prepared to make a decision about the test and had increased perceived benefits of MSI testing. </p>

4977<p id="_358" tabindex="-1">

4978Other innovative strategies include educational materials and interactive computer technology. In one study, a 13-page color communication aid using a diverse format for conveying risk, including graphic representations and verbal descriptions, was developed.[<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

497911</a>

4980] The authors evaluated the influence of the communication aid in 27 women who were at high risk of having a <em class="gene-name">

4981BRCA1</em>

4982/<em class="gene-name">

4983BRCA2</em>

4984 pathogenic variant. They compared these women with a sample of 107 women who received standard genetic counseling. Improvements in genetic knowledge and accuracy of risk perception were documented in those who had read the aid. There were no differences in anxiety or depression between groups. Personalized, interactive electronic materials have also been developed to aid in genetic education and counseling.[<a href="#cit/section_7.12">

498512</a>

4986,<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

498713</a>

4988] In one study, an interactive computer education program available prior to the genetic counseling session was compared with genetic counseling alone in women undergoing counseling for <em class="gene-name">

4989BRCA1</em>

4990/<em class="gene-name">

4991BRCA2</em>

4992 testing.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

499313</a>

4994] Use of the computer program prior to genetic counseling reduced face-time with the genetic counselor, particularly for those at lower risk of a <em class="gene-name">

4995BRCA1</em>

4996/<em class="gene-name">

4997BRCA2</em>

4998 pathogenic variant. Many of the counselors reported that their client&#8217;s use of the computer program allowed them to be more efficient and to reallocate time spent in the sessions to clients&#8217; unique concerns.</p>

4999<p id="_359" tabindex="-1">

5000Videoconferencing is an innovative strategy to facilitate genetic counseling sessions with clients who cannot travel to specialized clinic settings. In 37 individuals in the United Kingdom, real-time video conferencing was compared with face-to-face counseling sessions; both methods were found to improve knowledge and reduce anxiety levels.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

500114</a>

5002] Similarly, teleconferencing sessions, in which the client and genetic specialists talked with each other in real time, were used in rural Maine communities [<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

500315</a>

5004] for pediatric genetic consults. These sessions were used to convey genetic information and developmental delays. These sessions resulted in comparable decision-making confidence and session satisfaction when contrasted with in-person consultations. An Australian study compared the experiences of 106 women who received hereditary breast and ovarian cancer (HBOC) genetic counseling via videoconferencing with the experiences of 89 women who received counseling face to face. Pre- and 1-month postcounseling assessments revealed no significant differences in knowledge gains, satisfaction, cancer-specific anxiety, generalized anxiety, depression, and perceived empathy of the genetic counselor.[<a href="#cit/section_7.18">

500518</a>

5006]</p>

5007</section>

5008<section id="_122">

5009<h3 id="_122_toc">

5010Posttest Education and Result Notification</h3>

5011<p id="_126" tabindex="-1">

5012Posttest counseling may include consideration of the implications of the test

5013results for other family members. It has been suggested that some individuals <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460124&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460124&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5014affected</a>

5015 by an inherited disorder agree to have genetic testing performed in order to acquire information that could be shared with family members. There is evidence that implementation of a follow-up counseling program with the <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460211&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460211&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5016proband</a>

5017, after test results are revealed, will significantly increase the proportion of relatives informed of their genetic risk. Follow-up counseling may include telephone conversations with the proband verifying which family members have been contacted and an offer to assist with conveying information to family members.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

501819</a>

5019] Some experts have suggested that if a test

5020result is positive, plans should be made at this time for the notification, education,

5021and counseling of other relatives based on the test result of the individual.

5022Written materials, brochures, or personal letters may aid people in informing

5023the appropriate relatives about genetic risk.

5024</p>

5025<p id="_127" tabindex="-1">

5026When a test result is negative, the posttest session may be briefer. It is important, however, to discuss genetic, medical, and psychological implications of

5027a negative result in a family with a known pathogenic variant. For example, it

5028is essential that the person understand that the general population risks for

5029relevant cancer types still apply; additionally, the person&#8217;s individual risk of cancer

5030may still be influenced by other risk factors and family history from

5031the other side of the person's family. Furthermore, people may feel

5032distress even when a test is negative. This outcome has been documented in the context of <em class="gene-name">

5033BRCA1</em>

5034/<em class="gene-name">

5035BRCA2</em>

5036 pathogenic variant testing [<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

503720</a>

5038] and may also be anticipated in other cancer

5039susceptibility testing. Posttest results discussion of such distress may

5040lead to referral for additional counseling in some cases.

5041</p>

5042<p id="_128" tabindex="-1">

5043Many individuals benefit from follow-up counseling and consultation with medical specialists after

5044disclosure of genetic test results. This provides an opportunity for further discussion of

5045feelings about their risk statuses, options for risk management (including screening and detection procedures), and implications of the test

5046results for other family members.

5047For example, a standard-of-care randomized controlled trial of 100 female <em class="gene-name">

5048BRCA1</em>

5049/<em class="gene-name">

5050BRCA2</em>

5051 carriers found positive psychological outcomes and improved sleep quality in those who participated in the 12-week Inquiry-Based Stress Reduction (IBSR) intervention. In this study, outcomes were measured 24 to 26 weeks after the IBSR intervention.[<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

505221</a>

5053]</p>

5054</section>

5055<section id="_1609">

5056<h3 id="_1609_toc">

5057Cascade Genetic Testing of Family Members</h3>

5058<p id="_1674" tabindex="-1">

5059<em>

5060<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=799496&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000799496&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5061Cascade genetic testing</a>

5062</em>

5063 refers to the process of offering genetic testing to biologic family members who are at risk of inheriting the pathogenic variant previously identified in their relative. The process is repeated as additional pathogenic variant carriers are identified within a family. Cascade testing provides the opportunity to identify <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460132&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460132&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5064carriers</a>

5065 of a pathogenic variant prior to cancer presentation, which allows opportunities for cancer prevention, early detection, risk reduction, and ultimately, improved health outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

506622</a>

5067] There is wide variation in how cascade testing is conducted internationally; this section currently focuses on cascade testing in the United States.</p>

5068<section id="_1676">

5069<h4 id="_1676_toc">

5070Uptake of cascade genetic testing</h4>

5071<p id="_1677" tabindex="-1">

5072The dissemination of genetic risk information, from the proband to at-risk relatives, is essential for the uptake of cascade testing. Traditionally, this has consisted of discussions regarding the importance of result dissemination to at-risk relatives identified through the proband&#8217;s <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5073pedigree</a>

5074; this may be supplemented with a letter or educational materials that the proband can use to facilitate disclosure to family members.[<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

507523</a>

5076,<a href="#cit/section_7.24">

507724</a>

5078]</p>

5079<p id="_1678" tabindex="-1">

5080Most studies evaluating the uptake of cascade testing have been done in either HBOC or Lynch syndrome. A systematic evidence review including both HBOC and Lynch syndrome studies found that notification of the family pathogenic variant by the proband in HBOC families ranged from 21% to 44%. <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460150&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460150&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5081First-degree relatives</a>

5082 (FDRs), females, and close family members were more likely to be informed. Testing rates varied depending on the study, with 15% to 57% undergoing genetic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

508325</a>

5084] In Lynch syndrome, studies reported higher rates of the proband providing variant notification ranging from 41% to 94%, with one study reporting 70% of FDRs undergoing testing.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

508525</a>

5086] However, another study of carriers of pathogenic variants in a gynecologic oncology clinic found high rates of disclosure (87% of FDRs) yet a relatively low uptake of testing, particularly for Lynch syndrome compared with HBOC (33% vs. 49%, respectively; <em>

5087P</em>

5088 = .02).[<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

508926</a>

5090]</p>

5091<section id="_1679">

5092<h5 id="_1679_toc">

5093Barriers to cascade genetic testing</h5>

5094<section id="_1680">

5095<h6 id="_1680_toc">

5096Communication barriers</h6>

5097<p id="_1681" tabindex="-1">

5098Emotional barriers found to influence communication of pathogenic variant results to family members include loss of contact and lack of a close emotional relationship,[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

509927</a>

5100] transmission of guilt,[<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

510128</a>

5102] anxiety about cancer risks in relatives,[<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

510328</a>

5104] concerns that family members would have difficulty understanding the results,[<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

510529</a>

5106] emotional difficulties for the relative receiving the information,[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

510727</a>

5108] and negative impacts on family relationships and dynamics.[<a href="#cit/section_7.30">

510930</a>

5110-<a href="#cit/section_7.35">

511135</a>

5112] Similarly, one study found that patients who reported comfort with discussing health information (<em>

5113P</em>

5114 = .012) and/or high communication strength within the family (<em>

5115P</em>

5116 = .05) were more likely to disclose positive genetic test results for HBOC and Lynch syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

511726</a>

5118] In a qualitative study that focused on family communication, ethnically diverse carriers of <em class="gene-name">

5119BRCA1</em>

5120/<em class="gene-name">

5121BRCA2</em>

5122 pathogenic variants received genetic counseling and testing through a county hospital or a tertiary cancer center. This had no influence on overall rates of disclosure of results to family members (73%). However, individuals of African American heritage and individuals of Asian or Pacific Islander heritage were less likely to disclose results (47% and 70%, respectively), when compared with individuals of White heritage (91%). Additionally, African American individuals were less likely to undergo testing (odds ratio, 0.16; 95% confidence interval, 0.06&#8211;0.40).[<a href="#cit/section_7.36">

512336</a>

5124]</p>

5125</section>

5126<section id="_1682">

5127<h6 id="_1682_toc">

5128Information interpretation</h6>

5129<p id="_1683" tabindex="-1">

5130In a retrospective study of FDRs of a proband with a pathogenic or likely pathogenic variant, 14% of FDRs who were given the information found the information very or somewhat hard to understand. FDR recall of the test result was concordant with the actual test result in 82% of cases; however, 10% were unable to recall the result.[<a href="#cit/section_7.37">

513137</a>

5132]</p>

5133</section>

5134</section>

5135</section>

5136<section id="_1684">

5137<h4 id="_1684_toc">

5138Strategies to facilitate cascade genetic testing</h4>

5139<p id="_1685" tabindex="-1">

5140Several strategies aimed at facilitating family dissemination and testing uptake have been studied. </p>

5141<section id="_1686">

5142<h5 id="_1686_toc">

5143Written material</h5>

5144<p id="_1687" tabindex="-1">

5145The Finland Lynch syndrome Registry studied reaching out to at-risk family members through letters. Family members (N = 446) at 50% risk of developing Lynch syndrome from 36 families were notified through letters, which offered genetic counseling and testing for the documented variant in the family. Of the 446 family members, 347 underwent genetic counseling, with 75% of the entire cohort (n = 334) opting for testing.[<a href="#cit/section_7.38">

514638</a>

5147]</p>

5148</section>

5149<section id="_1688">

5150<h5 id="_1688_toc">

5151Group counseling</h5>

5152<p id="_1689" tabindex="-1">

5153Establishment of the Family Information Service at Creighton University provided group counseling sessions to at-risk relatives attending an education session. Group sizes ranged from 15 to 75 individuals and sessions were conducted by genetic nurses and counselors in a location near family residences. While an uptake rate of testing was not reported, these sessions considerably reduced the one-on-one health care provider time, thus increasing the capacity of the genetic clinic.[<a href="#cit/section_7.39">

515439</a>

5155]</p>

5156</section>

5157<section id="_1690">

5158<h5 id="_1690_toc">

5159Proband training</h5>

5160<p id="_1691" tabindex="-1">

5161Some groups have studied strategies to prepare probands to disseminate genetic test results to at-risk relatives. A randomized controlled study explored training on a six-step communication strategy that consisted of identifying at-risk relatives, selecting the communication method, assessing family member knowledge, sharing the result, responding to reactions, and providing genetic counseling resources. No significant difference in the rate of genetic test result dissemination between intervention (n = 137) and control groups (n = 112) was detected.[<a href="#cit/section_7.40">

516240</a>

5163]</p>

5164<p id="_1692" tabindex="-1">

5165A Netherlands group explored the feasibility of a two-phased telephone motivational interviewing intervention conducted by five trained psychosocial workers. Consultands (n = 144) included those with at least one relative eligible for genetic testing or cancer screening. Phase 1 determined the agenda, confirmed which family members needed to be informed with the proband, and explored current and planned result-sharing mechanisms. Phase 2 focused on sharing certain information, building motivation and self-efficacy, and brainstorming solutions to dissemination barriers. Outcomes showed that consultands found this strategy feasible and acceptable. A randomized study is under way to evaluate whether this intervention increases information dissemination.[<a href="#cit/section_7.41">

516641</a>

5167]</p>

5168<p id="_1693" tabindex="-1">

5169An Australian randomized controlled trial evaluated the impact of additional telephone genetic counseling support in individuals who had been diagnosed with a genetic condition, had a child diagnosed with a genetic condition, or were found to be carriers of a pathogenic variant. Additional telephone counseling in the intervention group (n = 45) occurred 2 to 3 times over 12 months, which was compared with controls (n = 50). No significant difference in relatives seeking genetic services was detected.[<a href="#cit/section_7.42">

517042</a>

5171]</p>

5172</section>

5173<section id="_1694">

5174<h5 id="_1694_toc">

5175Low- or no-cost genetic testing</h5>

5176<p id="_1695" tabindex="-1">

5177One study explored free genetic testing for at-risk relatives. In a <em class="gene-name">

5178BRCA1</em>

5179/<em class="gene-name">

5180BRCA2</em>

5181 pathogenic variant cohort (n = 115) eligible for free at-risk family member testing, 77% disclosed results to all at-risk family members. Only 60% of FDRs and 47% of more-distant relatives underwent genetic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_7.43">

518243</a>

5183]</p>

5184<p id="_1696" tabindex="-1">

5185Genetic testing laboratories are also exploring ways to reduce barriers to testing for family members, given that the uptake of cascade testing is low.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

518625</a>

5187,<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

518826</a>

5189] Some laboratories now offer low-cost self-pay testing or no-cost genetic testing to FDRs within a specified time period (e.g., 90 or more days) of a pathogenic or likely pathogenic variant being identified in a family member.[<a href="#cit/section_7.44">

519044</a>

5191,<a href="#cit/section_7.45">

519245</a>

5193] Outcomes from these efforts have not yet been reported. </p>

5194<p id="_1868" tabindex="-1">

5195One study performed cancer predisposition genetic testing on the basis of a diagnosis of a solid tumor cancer in patients unselected for family history or guideline-specified testing. In individuals found to harbor a pathogenic variant, all biological family members of the probands were offered no-cost testing for a 3-month period. Of the eligible families, 17.6% had one or more family members undergo genetic testing. A median of two individuals per family were tested (range, 1&#8211;14), although the total number of family members eligible for testing was not reported.[<a href="#cit/section_7.46">

519646</a>

5197]</p>

5198</section>

5199<section id="_1697">

5200<h5 id="_1697_toc">

5201Online direct-to-consumer testing</h5>

5202<p id="_1698" tabindex="-1">

5203One direct-to-consumer cancer genetic testing laboratory that performs only a 30-gene panel offered reduced-cost ($50) testing to FDRs of individuals found to harbor a pathogenic variant. Individuals were emailed information about the family testing program, and those interested who applied (n = 1,101) identified the at-risk FDRs and provided their contact information. The laboratory sent emails to FDRs inviting them to undergo testing for the 30-gene panel, which included the pathogenic variants found in their relatives. In 1 year of follow-up, 48% of the invited FDRs underwent testing. Only 12% of FDRs who were tested and found to have a pathogenic variant continued cascade testing of their own FDRs. Notably, 5% of FDRs were found to have pathogenic variants in genes other than the ones found in their family members. Additionally, 16.8% of FDRs were found to have a variant of uncertain significance.[<a href="#cit/section_7.47">

520447</a>

5205]</p>

5206</section>

5207<section id="_1699">

5208<h5 id="_1699_toc">

5209Direct provider-to-family testing</h5>

5210<p id="_1700" tabindex="-1">

5211A systematic evidence review studied the dissemination of genetic test results to the probands' family members, using contact information that was provided by probands. Results showed that the number of probands tested was higher than the number of relatives who had been notified of the probands' results. Four studies also found that relatives did not understand the genetic test results; however, when sufficiently informed by a health care provider, most opted to be tested.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

521225</a>

5213]</p>

5214<p id="_1732" tabindex="-1">

5215Similarly, in a study with 30 probands who had a pathogenic variant, 114 at-risk relatives were identified. Ultimately, probands gave permission for the study coordinators to contact 102 of the at-risk relatives. Of those relatives, 95 of 102 (93%) were successfully contacted by a member of the genetics team, 92 (97%) agreed to genetic counseling, 82 (86%) agreed to testing, and 66 (70%) completed testing for the variant in the family.[<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

521622</a>

5217] </p>

5218<p id="_1860" tabindex="-1">

5219When done in a clinical setting, there may be other considerations, such as billing and institutional privacy regulations, that need to be investigated before pursuing direct contact with family members.</p>

5220</section>

5221<section id="_1701">

5222<h5 id="_1701_toc">

5223Chatbots</h5>

5224<p id="_1702" tabindex="-1">

5225Chatbots use artificial intelligence to create an online avatar that can speak back and forth with users, simulating real conversations on the basis of pre-established text-based dialogues.[<a href="#cit/section_7.48">

522648</a>

5227] Chatbots are actively being explored as a mechanism to respond to family member cascade testing queries. </p>

5228</section>

5229<p id="_1703" tabindex="-1">

5230In summary, these studies document that relying on probands to notify family members of genetic risk has limitations affecting both information dissemination and the uptake of testing. No one strategy has been shown to be optimal.[<a href="#cit/section_7.49">

523149</a>

5232]</p>

5233</section>

5234<section id="_1704">

5235<h4 id="_1704_toc">

5236Ethical, legal, and social issues</h4>

5237<p id="_1705" tabindex="-1">

5238Ethical, legal, and social issues related to cascade testing, such as duty to warn and disclosure to at-risk relatives, are discussed in the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_240">

5239Ethical, Legal, and Social Implications</a>

5240 section of this summary.</p>

5241</section>

5242</section>

5438[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

5439</li>

5440</ol>

5441</div>

5442

5443 </section>

5495<section id="_1489">

5496<h3 id="_1489_toc">

5497Modalities of Genetic Counseling</h3>

5498<p id="_1495" tabindex="-1">

5499Cancer risk assessment counseling is a multistep process that traditionally included an in-person pretest and posttest counseling session. In an effort to overcome access barriers, other modalities have been implemented, including group sessions, telephone counseling, and online <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5500genetic counseling</a>

5501 using remote videoconferencing, which is often referred to as <em>

5502telegenetics</em>

5503.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

55041</a>

5505-<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

550610</a>

5507] Of these other modalities, only telephone counseling has been examined for <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=768568&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000768568&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5508noninferiority</a>

5509 against in-person genetic counseling in a randomized controlled trial.[<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

551011</a>

5511-<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

551214</a>

5513] </p>

5514<section id="_1490">

5515<h4 id="_1490_toc">

5516Telephone genetic counseling</h4>

5517<p id="_1572" tabindex="-1">

5518A systematic review identified 13 published studies that used a randomized controlled trial design to compare pretest and posttest outcomes for in-person genetic counseling with telephone counseling. Knowledge and psychosocial outcomes (e.g., distress) were found to be noninferior, equivalent, or not statistically significant between telephone counseling and in-person counseling. Two studies demonstrated lower testing intention or uptake among participants who received telephone counseling. The majority of studies also found no difference in satisfaction; however, two studies demonstrated higher satisfaction among individuals who received telephone compared with those who received in-person genetic counseling.[<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

551914</a>

5520] A subsequent study examined several dimensions of patient perceptions of genetic counseling among participants of a randomized trial of telephone versus in-person genetic counseling.[<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

552115</a>

5522] In the 2-week period after their pretest genetic counseling appointment, participants who had telephone-based counseling were more likely to rate it as convenient; however, they also reported lower levels of support and emotional recognition by the counselor. There were no differences in overall satisfaction. Exploratory analysis demonstrated that racial and ethnic minority participants reported lower perceptions of counselor support with in-person counseling when compared with telephone counseling. The opposite was observed for non-Hispanic White participants. Additional studies are needed to confirm these findings given the small sample size. (The studies were conducted prior to the adoption of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=775581&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000775581&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5523multigene panel testing</a>

5524.)</p>

5525<p id="_1573" tabindex="-1">

5526Another group reported results of a study in which all participants (N = 1,178) received in-person pretest counseling at one of five participating sites. Those participants willing to be randomized had their results disclosed by telephone (n = 401) or in person (n = 418). Notably, 30% of participants in this study had multigene panel testing. In this trial, telephone disclosure was noninferior to in-person results disclosure when comparing primary psychosocial outcomes (e.g., general and state anxiety). In primary analysis, knowledge did not meet the threshold of noninferiority without imputing missing data. Secondary outcomes related to cancer distress, depression, uncertainty, satisfaction with genetic testing, and behavioral intentions for risk management strategies were not statistically significant between groups.[<a href="#cit/section_8.16">

552716</a>

5528]</p>

5529</section>

5530<section id="_1492">

5531<h4 id="_1492_toc">

5532Video-assisted genetic counseling</h4>

5533<p id="_1493" tabindex="-1">

5534Studies have also examined the use of online genetic counseling using remote videoconferencing (telegenetics) as an alternative to in-person genetic counseling and demonstrated increases in patient knowledge, high levels of satisfaction, and minimal negative psychosocial outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_8.17">

553517</a>

5536-<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

553720</a>

5538] </p>

5539</section>

5540</section>

5541<section id="_1613">

5542<h3 id="_1613_toc">

5543Genetic Service Delivery Models</h3>

5544<p id="_1620" tabindex="-1">

5545Emerging approaches to delivering clinical genetic services have been examined to facilitate greater access to genetic counseling and testing. These approaches have been utilized to streamline the process by which high-risk or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460124&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460124&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5546affected</a>

5547 individuals are identified and referred to genetic services. These service delivery models vary in the processes by which patients receive genetic education, counseling, and testing, with genetic counseling increasingly taking place only after genetic testing has already occurred.</p>

5548<p id="_1670" tabindex="-1">

5549Several factors have contributed to the provision of genetic testing without pretest genetic counseling. These factors include: (1) expansion of genetic testing criteria, resulting in increased demand for genetic testing; (2) more indications for testing at the time of cancer diagnosis, given that the identification of a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000783960&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5550pathogenic variant</a>

5551 may affect treatment options (e.g., poly [ADP-ribose] polymerase [PARP] inhibitors in <em class="gene-name">

5552BRCA1</em>

5553/<em class="gene-name">

5554BRCA2</em>

5555 positive patients with metastatic human epidermal growth factor receptor 2&#8211;negative breast cancer, ovarian cancer, or pancreatic cancer); (3) increasing numbers of patients who undergo tumor genomic testing to guide treatment, which may be followed by confirmatory <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5556germline</a>

5557 testing; (4) increasing availability of universal testing (e.g., for ovarian, pancreatic, and prostate cancer patients) and consumer-directed genetic testing. Some indications have resulted in patients being offered genetic testing by their health care providers in a nongenetics environment (e.g., by primary care providers [PCP], surgeons, or oncologists), which may then be followed by posttest result interpretation and counseling by the provider or via a genetics specialist. </p>

5558<section id="_1927">

5559<h4 id="_1927_toc">

5560Targeted outreach and testing of high-risk populations</h4>

5561<p id="_1928" tabindex="-1">

5562High-risk populations, such as those of Ashkenazi Jewish descent, may be offered genetic testing through targeted outreach and population screening programs without pretest counseling or a streamlined education process that provides written or other materials.[<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

556321</a>

5564-<a href="#cit/section_8.25">

556525</a>

5566] In an Israeli study that offered population screening via proactive outreach and self-referral, <em class="gene-name">

5567BRCA1</em>

5568/<em class="gene-name">

5569BRCA2</em>

5570 testing uptake was 67%, and satisfaction with the population screening approach was greater than 90% at both 1 week and 6 months posttesting.[<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

557121</a>

5572] Posttest genetic counseling was offered in person to <em class="gene-name">

5573BRCA1</em>

5574/<em class="gene-name">

5575BRCA2</em>

5576 carriers with significant family histories of cancer. Letters detailing test results and general screening recommendations were sent to noncarriers with limited family histories. Posttest counseling compliance was 100% for carriers and 87% for noncarriers with suggestive family histories. Notably, gender differences in compliance were found among noncarriers (89% for women vs. 78% for men; <em>

5577P</em>

5578 = .01).[<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

557921</a>

5580]</p>

5581<p id="_1929" tabindex="-1">

5582A U.S. study deployed community outreach and online interactive pretest education (via a chatbot and video education) for individuals with Ashkenazi Jewish ancestry (i.e., had at least one grandparent who was Ashkenazi Jewish). Participants were then offered genetic testing for the three Ashkenazi Jewish founder pathogenic variants in <em class="gene-name">

5583BRCA1/BRCA2</em>

5584. The study reported a genetic testing uptake rate of 79% among participants.[<a href="#cit/section_8.25">

558525</a>

5586] However, only 8% of those who visited the study website enrolled. This suggests that those who enrolled after pretest education were motivated and likely to proceed with genetic testing. Like in the Israeli study, genetic test results were delivered to participants by a clinician (cancer specialist or the participant's PCP) when results were positive or negative and when the participants had an increased risk of carrying a <em class="gene-name">

5587BRCA1/BRCA2</em>

5588 pathogenic variant (based on a familial <em class="gene-name">

5589BRCA1/BRCA2</em>

5590 pathogenic variant or significant family histories of cancer). Negative test results were mailed to all other participants. Of note, participants who tested negative and had significant family histories displayed low adherence to study recommendations for additional genetic testing.</p>

5591</section>

5592<section id="_1930">

5593<h4 id="_1930_toc">

5594Universal germline genetic testing approaches in oncology</h4>

5595<p id="_1931" tabindex="-1">

5596Universal germline genetic testing is now standard practice for patients with certain cancers, such as epithelial ovarian, exocrine pancreatic, and metastatic prostate cancers.[<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

559726</a>

5598] For more information on hereditary prostate cancer genetic testing, see the <a href="/types/prostate/hp/prostate-genetics-pdq#_1520">

5599Clinical Application of Genetic Testing for Inherited Prostate Cancer</a>

5600 section in Genetics of Prostate Cancer. For more information on hereditary ovarian cancer genetic testing, see the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_3079">

5601Indications for hereditary breast and gynecologic cancers genetic testing</a>

5602 section in Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers.</p>

5603<p id="_1932" tabindex="-1">

5604Long-standing guidelines for universal germline genetic testing exist for patients with ovarian cancer. National guidelines in the United States [<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

560526</a>

5606,<a href="#cit/section_8.27">

560727</a>

5608] and international guidelines [<a href="#cit/section_8.28">

560928</a>

5610,<a href="#cit/section_8.29">

561129</a>

5612] recommend offering genetic testing to all women with ovarian cancer. There are two primary reasons for the endorsement of universal testing in this patient population, given that up to 15% of ovarian cancer patients harbor a pathogenic variant in <em class="gene-name">

5613BRCA1</em>

5614 or <em class="gene-name">

5615BRCA2</em>

5616: (1) to identify patients who may benefit from targeted therapy with a PARP inhibitor, such as olaparib; and (2) to facilitate the identification of at-risk relatives through the process of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=799495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000799495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5617cascade testing</a>

5618.[<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

561926</a>

5620,<a href="#cit/section_8.27">

562127</a>

5622,<a href="#cit/section_8.30">

562330</a>

5624-<a href="#cit/section_8.32">

562532</a>

5626]</p>

5627<p id="_1933" tabindex="-1">

5628In response, various efforts have been made to implement universal germline testing for cancer patients, including embedding genetic services into patients' oncology and oncology-mediated care.[<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

562933</a>

5630,<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

563134</a>

5632]</p>

5633<section id="_1934">

5634<h5 id="_1934_toc">

5635Embedded genetic services</h5>

5636<p id="_1935" tabindex="-1">

5637Studies have examined the impact of embedding a cancer genetic counselor on site at gynecologic oncology clinics to increase referral to genetics and increase the use of genetic counseling among affected women.[<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

563833</a>

5639,<a href="#cit/section_8.35">

564035</a>

5641-<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

564238</a>

5643] Referral rates and genetic counseling uptake improved (up to 85%) following the incorporation of an on-site genetic counselor. Studies reported that patients had shorter appointment visit lengths with the genetic counselor.[<a href="#cit/section_8.35">

564435</a>

5645] Patients also had shorter intervals between being referred to the clinic and completing their genetic consultations.[<a href="#cit/section_8.36">

564636</a>

5647-<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

564838</a>

5649]</p>

5650</section>

5651<section id="_1936">

5652<h5 id="_1936_toc">

5653Oncology-mediated or mainstreamed models of care</h5>

5654<p id="_1937" tabindex="-1">

5655Other universal testing efforts have focused on oncology-mediated or mainstreamed models of care wherein oncology physicians provide pretest education and counseling, obtain <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5656informed consent</a>

5657, conduct genetic testing, and return negative genetic test results, with possible triaging the return of positive or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=556493&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000556493&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5658variants of unknown significance</a>

5659 results to genetic counselors.[<a href="#cit/section_8.37">

566037</a>

5661,<a href="#cit/section_8.39">

566239</a>

5663] One study reported that patients were highly satisfied (&gt;99%) with oncologist-led genetic counseling and testing.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

566434</a>

5665,<a href="#cit/section_8.39">

566639</a>

5667-<a href="#cit/section_8.41">

566841</a>

5669]</p>

5670<p id="_1938" tabindex="-1">

5671A systematic review described the results from 11 studies of oncology provider&#8211;led germline cancer genetic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

567234</a>

5673] Most studies (10/11) involved <em class="gene-name">

5674BRCA1</em>

5675/<em class="gene-name">

5676BRCA2</em>

5677 testing in the context of breast or ovarian cancer. Overall, oncologist-led genetic testing resulted in a shorter turnaround time for test results, a higher genetic testing uptake, and similar psychological outcomes when compared with genetic testing conducted via genetic counseling referral. Patients also reported valuing the convenience and continuity of care with a familiar, trusted provider. Patient satisfaction with oncologist-led counseling and testing was high. Oncologists have also reported favorable experiences regarding this counseling approach and find it acceptable.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

567834</a>

5679,<a href="#cit/section_8.41">

568041</a>

5681] However, some studies reported that patients who received pretest genetic counseling by a nongenetics provider were likely to remember fewer elements that were discussed during the informed consent process. Furthermore, some patients did not recall having a pretest informed consent discussion.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

568234</a>

5683] Other studies have similarly reported that patients learned less and gave less favorable evaluations regarding the helpfulness of the information that they received from oncology-mediated care.[<a href="#cit/section_8.40">

568440</a>

5685]</p>

5686</section>

5687</section>

5688<section id="_1939">

5689<h4 id="_1939_toc">

5690Mainstreamed models in primary care</h4>

5691<p id="_1940" tabindex="-1">

5692Mainstreaming approaches are also being deployed in non-oncology practices. For example, in a study of five community obstetrics and gynecologic practices, clinicians were trained in hereditary cancer risk assessment. Clinics also modified patient screening practices and workflows. After 8 weeks of deploying the modified workflow, 92.8% (3,811/4,107) of patients were assessed for hereditary cancer risk, and 23.8% (906/3,811) of those assessed met National Comprehensive Cancer Network (NCCN) guidelines for genetic testing. Among those who met NCCN guidelines, 89.7% (813/906) were offered genetic testing by their clinicians. Overall, 26.7% (219/813) of the women provided samples for testing and 20% (165/813) received their results. Satisfaction with the process was high (~97%).[<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

569342</a>

5694] Notably, patients who were offered genetic testing during their clinic visits (i.e., point-of-care testing) were more likely to submit a sample (98.1%) than those who were referred to a nurse practitioner to have genetic testing within 2 weeks (40.7%). Results in this study were delivered by the treating clinician.[<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

569542</a>

5696]</p>

5697</section>

5698<section id="_1941">

5699<h4 id="_1941_toc">

5700Triaged services using nongenetics providers</h4>

5701<p id="_1942" tabindex="-1">

5702Nongenetics providers who receive training in cancer genetics are increasingly using triaged models to increase access to cancer genetics services. These providers may be engaged at different points along the risk assessment, counseling, and testing time line.[<a href="#cit/section_8.43">

570343</a>

5704-<a href="#cit/section_8.45">

570545</a>

5706] In one example, nurses were trained to provide basic risk assessment and offer <em class="gene-name">

5707BRCA</em>

5708 testing to patients in an effort to increase access to genetic service providers in rural settings.[<a href="#cit/section_8.44">

570944</a>

5710] Family histories collected via a paper screener administered in mammography or oncology sites were reviewed and triaged by the genetic counselor based on risk status, and subsequent counseling (regarding risk) was provided by either the nurse or the genetic counselor. A fourfold increase in the number of patients seen at the site was observed over a 2-year period. In another study, patient intake workflow was modified to include genetic counseling assistants. In this study, genetic counseling assistants provided pretest education, performed informed consent for testing, and ordered genetic testing for new patients with pancreatic cancer, whereas genetic counselors only reviewed and returned test results.[<a href="#cit/section_8.45">

571145</a>

5712] The genetic testing rate increased from 19% to 71% after the clinic began using genetic counseling assistants in their workflow. </p>

5713</section>

5714</section>
