5954<section id="_1738">

5955<h3 id="_1738_toc">

5956Duty to Warn: Considerations About Providers&#8217; Disclosure of Patient&#8217;s Genetic Information to At-Risk Relatives</h3>

5957<p id="_1739" tabindex="-1">

5958The identification of hereditary cancer risks in patients, through a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044868&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5959pedigree</a>

5960-based approach, clinical diagnosis, and/or the results of genetic testing, has implications for both patients and their biological family members. One of the major components of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044961&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5961genetic counseling</a>

5962, as recommended by many professional medical societies, is to inform patients about <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460148&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460148&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5963familial</a>

5964 risk and to encourage discussion with relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

59651</a>

5966-<a href="#cit/section_9.6">

59676</a>

5968] For more information about disclosing genetic test results to relatives, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1684">

5969Strategies to facilitate cascade genetic testing</a>

5970 section or the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_964">

5971Family communication about genetic testing and hereditary risk</a>

5972 section in Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers.</p>

5973<p id="_1740" tabindex="-1">

5974When patients do not inform their at-risk relatives about potentially actionable genetic risks (e.g., pathogenic variants in high-risk <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000045693&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5975genes</a>

5976 such as <em class="gene-name">

5977BRCA1</em>

5978 or <em class="gene-name">

5979BRCA2</em>

5980) or do not give their providers permission to share these results, providers may face a dilemma about their <em>

5981duty to warn</em>

5982 the relatives. There are several ethical and legal considerations that factor into decisions about what responsibility, if any, providers have to directly inform at-risk relatives about hereditary cancer risks. </p>

5983<p id="_1741" tabindex="-1">

5984If a provider is considering overriding patient confidentiality or consent to directly notify relatives about genetic information, it is important to consider a consultation with one or more of the following: ethicist, ethics committee, legal counsel, privacy officer, and, if applicable, institutional review board to assure adherence to local ethical standards and legal, regulatory, and privacy requirements. </p>

5985<section id="_1742">

5986<h4 id="_1742_toc">

5987Duty to warn considerations</h4>

5988<p id="_1753" tabindex="-1">

5989Patients are encouraged to provide at-risk relatives with genetic testing results that reveal pathogenic or likely pathogenic variants. This is especially encouraged when patients have moderate-to-high risk variants that are associated with increased cancer risk and may guide decisions about cancer <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000046171&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

5990screening</a>

5991 and risk reduction behaviors.</p>

5992<p id="_1861" tabindex="-1">

5993If a patient with a pathogenic variant declines to notify at-risk relatives, there is debate about whether his/her health care provider has a duty to warn these family members.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

59947</a>

5995] The concept of duty to warn has sparked much discussion in the medical community. This issue has been examined from legal and ethical perspectives. Medical organizations have also published expert opinions on a provider's duty to warn at-risk relatives. For example, the American Society of Human Genetics (ASHG) outlined circumstances in which it could be permissible for a health care provider to consider directly contacting and notifying at-risk relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

59965</a>

5997] (The ASHG statement mentioned above was published in 1998, prior to the publication of HIPAA's Privacy Rule. This ASHG statement has not been updated since.) Such circumstances may include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

59985</a>

5999]</p>

6000<div class="pdq-content-list">

6001<ol id="_1744">

6002<li>

6003There is a high likelihood of harm if relatives are not warned.</li>

6004<li>

6005The threat of disease risk is foreseeable and imminent.</li>

6006<li>

6007The disease or condition is preventable or treatable.</li>

6008<li>

6009The patient, despite encouragement, refuses to inform family members.</li>

6010<li>

6011The harm of nondisclosure is greater than the harm of disclosure.</li>

6012<li>

6013The at-risk relative(s) are identifiable and may include first-, second-, and third-degree relatives.</li>

6014</ol>

6015</div>

6016<p id="_1862" tabindex="-1">

6017It is uncommon for providers to breach a patient's confidentiality by informing at-risk relatives about a genetic test result.</p>

6018<p id="_1819" tabindex="-1">

6019It is possible that the patient refuses to inform family members but gives permission for their provider to directly contact their at-risk relatives. In such instances, it is important to document the patient&#8217;s consent and consider the optimum methods for communicating with relatives, as well as the provider&#8217;s resources to follow-up with such requests.[<a href="#cit/section_9.8">

60208</a>

6021] In addition, even when the patient consents to the provider contacting relatives directly, it is important to consider a consultation with one or more of the following: ethicist, ethics committee, legal counsel, privacy officer, and, if applicable, institutional review board to assure adherence to local ethical standards and legal, regulatory, and privacy requirements.</p>

6022<p id="_1757" tabindex="-1">

6023Many providers may not know the identity of at-risk relatives. Providers also may not be able to obtain or confirm a relative's contact information if it was obtained from publicly available resources. Thus, duty to warn dilemmas arise most frequently when a provider or a provider's medical system treats more than one individual from the same family or when a provider has already had contact with a patient's family members.</p>

6024<p id="_1820" tabindex="-1">

6025For more information about informing at-risk relatives, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1684">

6026Strategies to facilitate cascade genetic testing</a>

6027 section or the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq#_964">

6028Family communication about genetic testing and hereditary risk</a>

6029 section in Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers.</p>

6030</section>

6031<section id="_1758">

6032<h4 id="_1758_toc">

6033Court holdings regarding duty to warn</h4>

6034<p id="_1759" tabindex="-1">

6035There are very few legal precedents that guide whether the duty to directly warn family members is the responsibility of the patient or the provider. The two most prominent cases related to hereditary cancer risk, <em>

6036Pate v Threlkel</em>

6037 (regarding medullary thyroid cancer) and <em>

6038Safer v Pack</em>

6039 (regarding familial adenomatous polyposis), are also dated (1995 and 1996, respectively) and may have the most relevance only in the states in which the cases were adjudicated (Florida and New Jersey, respectively).[<a href="#cit/section_9.9">

60409</a>

6041,<a href="#cit/section_9.10">

604210</a>

6043] These cases and the potential implications of their holdings are discussed elsewhere.[<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

604411</a>

6045-<a href="#cit/section_9.15">

604615</a>

6047]</p>

6048</section>

6049<section id="_1760">

6050<h4 id="_1760_toc">

6051Guidelines and legal/legislative frameworks regarding duty to warn</h4>

6052<p id="_1761" tabindex="-1">

6053In deciding whether there may be a duty to warn at-risk relatives about hereditary risk, it is important to balance the bioethical constructs of beneficence and nonmaleficence (providing benefit and avoiding harm, respectively) and autonomy with other factors such as professional societies&#8217; recommendations, state and federal legislation, and court holdings from various states. The definition of <em>

6054genetic information</em>

6055 (related to hereditary risk) may vary depending on the legal case and the language used in state and federal legislation, although it generally encompasses genetic testing, as well as family history information. The information below pertains to guidance in the United States, as there is variability in international perspectives and policies.[<a href="#cit/section_9.16">

605616</a>

6057-<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

605819</a>

6059]</p>

6060<section id="_1762">

6061<h5 id="_1762_toc">

6062Professional society guidelines regarding duty to warn</h5>

6063<p id="_1763" tabindex="-1">

6064Many professional medical societies and government agencies have published their positions and recommendations on communication between a health care provider and a patient's relatives in regard to disclosure of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=256553&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000256553&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6065genetic risk</a>

6066.

6067Several organizations such as the <a href="https://www.ama-assn.org" title="https://www.ama-assn.org">

6068American Medical Association</a>

6069, <a href="https://beta.asco.org/" title="https://beta.asco.org/">

6070American Society of Clinical Oncology</a>

6071,<a href="https://www.nsgc.org/" title="https://www.nsgc.org/">

6072 National Society of Genetic Counselors</a>

6073, and the <a href="https://www.isong.org/" title="https://www.isong.org/">

6074International Society of Nurses in Genetics</a>

6075 recommend that patients who undergo genetic testing disclose the information directly to their at-risk relatives and do not recommend provider notification of relatives without consent.

6076However, the American Society of Human Genetics, which encourages individuals to notify their relatives directly, also provides an explication for criteria where it may be ethically permissible for providers to directly notify at-risk relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

60775</a>

6078]</p>

6079</section>

6080</section>

6081<section id="_1775">

6082<h4 id="_1775_toc">

6083Federal and state laws regarding duty to warn</h4>

6084<p id="_1776" tabindex="-1">

6085At the federal level, strict nondisclosure policies govern private health information.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

60867</a>

6087,<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

608811</a>

6089] The Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) applies to protected health information in living and deceased individuals.[<a href="#cit/section_9.20">

609020</a>

6091] Specifically, the Standards for Privacy of Individually Identifiable Health Information (Privacy Rule, in effect since 2003), states that it is permissible to disclose health information without consent when the public interest is at risk;[<a href="#cit/section_9.21">

609221</a>

6093,<a href="#cit/section_9.22">

609422</a>

6095] therefore, under certain conditions, exceptions to the nondisclosure policy include the following:</p>

6096<div class="pdq-content-list">

6097<ol id="_1777">

6098<li>

6099There is serious or imminent threat to the health or safety of a person or the public.</li>

6100<li>

6101The threat constitutes an imminent, serious threat to an identifiable third party.</li>

6102<li>

6103The physician has the capacity to avert significant harm.</li>

6104</ol>

6105</div>

6106<p id="_1778" tabindex="-1">

6107In addition, HIPAA contains a <em>

6108minimum necessary standard</em>

6109, which means that entities that are subject to such regulations can only request and receive information relevant to a specific purpose.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

61107</a>

6111,<a href="#cit/section_9.23">

611223</a>

6113] The type and extent of genetic information that can be released to relatives depends on several factors. These factors include why the genetic data were obtained (i.e., for research purposes or for public health purposes) and if the genetic data have the potential to support medical decision making for cancer treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

61147</a>

6115,<a href="#cit/section_9.23">

611623</a>

6117] For example, in some instances it may be permissible for the physician of a genetically tested patient to share results with a relative&#8217;s physician if they are relevant to the relative's management recommendations.[<a href="#cit/section_9.23">

611823</a>

6119] The interpretation of this standard relates to disclosure to another provider, not the at-risk relatives directly. </p>

6120<p id="_1779" tabindex="-1">

6121At the state level, there is significant variability in statutes as they relate to genetic privacy and when, how, by, and to whom genetic information can be released.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

61227</a>

6123] The National Human Genome Research Institute at the National Institutes of Health maintains the <a href="https://www.genome.gov/about-genomics/policy-issues/Genome-Statute-Legislation-Database" title="https://www.genome.gov/about-genomics/policy-issues/Genome-Statute-Legislation-Database">

6124Genome Statute and Legislative Database</a>

6125, which is updated regularly. </p>

6126<p id="_1780" tabindex="-1">

6127If there is a question about whether it is appropriate to breach patient confidentiality to warn relatives, it is important to review these regulations, as well as federal and case law with an ethicist, ethics committee, legal counsel, and/or privacy officer to ensure adherence to local ethical standards and legal, regulatory, and privacy requirements. </p>

6128</section>

6129<section id="_1769">

6130<h4 id="_1769_toc">

6131Duty to warn considerations in deceased individuals</h4>

6132<p id="_1770" tabindex="-1">

6133The section above primarily addresses the duty to warn relatives when a living patient is unwilling to do so. However, concerns also exist about disclosure of genetic testing results from deceased individuals. This concern has arisen in research contexts related to targeted research findings (i.e., findings directly related to the study at hand) or secondary findings, in biobanks, and in clinical contexts.[<a href="#cit/section_9.24">

613424</a>

6135-<a href="#cit/section_9.27">

613627</a>

6137] Pragmatic tools for returning research results are available elsewhere.[<a href="#cit/section_9.28">

613828</a>

6139]</p>

6140<p id="_1771" tabindex="-1">

6141In clinical practice, the duty to warn about genetic testing results in a deceased individual has arisen after such testing is performed as part of an autopsy (e.g., revealing an inherited cause for sudden cardiac death).[<a href="#cit/section_9.29">

614229</a>

6143,<a href="#cit/section_9.30">

614430</a>

6145] However, in the clinical oncology setting, the question about providers disclosing a decedent&#8217;s test result to at-risk relatives may occur in several contexts. Examples include the following:</p>

6146<div class="pdq-content-list">

6147<ol id="_1876">

6148<li>

6149When cancer patients die before <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000460154&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6150germline</a>

6151 genetic testing results became available or before results were disclosed to them, and the result is positive for a pathogenic or likely pathogenic variant in a high-risk gene such as <em class="gene-name">

6152TP53</em>

6153, <em class="gene-name">

6154BRCA1</em>

6155, <em class="gene-name">

6156BRCA2</em>

6157, or those associated with Lynch syndrome.</li>

6158<li>

6159When a variant of uncertain significance is upgraded to a likely or known pathogenic variant, and the patient was not alive to receive the updated information.</li>

6160<li>

6161When at-risk relatives request the decedent&#8217;s testing result to make more informed testing decisions for themselves.</li>

6162</ol>

6163</div>

6164<p id="_1772" tabindex="-1">

6165In anticipation of these possible scenarios, some genetics providers may ask patients to sign a form designating which individuals can access their genetic testing results.[<a href="#cit/section_9.28">

616628</a>

6167,<a href="#cit/section_9.31">

616831</a>

6169] This form can specify whether this disclosure can occur after death, regardless of whether the patient had received the results. The form contains the relatives&#8217; full names, relationships to the patient, postal addresses, and if possible, mobile phone numbers and email addresses. NCCN recommends discussing the release of genetic test results to relatives during pretest counseling in the event that the patient dies or becomes incapacitated.[<a href="#cit/section_9.32">

617032</a>

6171]</p>

6172<p id="_1773" tabindex="-1">

6173HIPAA is a federal law that applies to protected health information in living and deceased individuals.[<a href="#cit/section_9.20">

617420</a>

6175] Unless the deceased individual had expressly stated that genetic testing results should not be shared, under HIPAA, after all relevant points of the law have been considered, it may be possible that this information could be shared with relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_9.20">

617620</a>

6177,<a href="#cit/section_9.33">

617833</a>

6179]</p>

6180<p id="_1774" tabindex="-1">

6181It is important to contact a privacy officer or a legal counsel before disclosing a deceased individual's test results to relatives. Privacy officers can determine if documentation is required (e.g., demonstrating who the personal representative is/was for the decedent), if specific regulations apply, and if the deceased individual provided permission to release and/or share genetic test results. In addition, an ethicist, an ethics committee, and, if applicable, an institutional review board may be consulted to ensure adherence to local ethical standards and legal, regulatory, and privacy requirements.</p>

6182</section>

6183<section id="_1781">

6184<h4 id="_1781_toc">

6185Duty to warn versus duty to rescue</h4>

6186<p id="_1782" tabindex="-1">

6187Traditionally, duty to warn refers to a provider's potential responsibility to notify a patient&#8217;s at-risk biological relatives, such as children and siblings, about a serious hereditary risk. However, more recently, questions have arisen about the duty to warn or duty to rescue the person being tested on the basis of the identification of secondary genomic findings, or genomic testing results that are potentially actionable but were not sought out as part of the indication for testing.[<a href="#cit/section_9.34">

618834</a>

6189]</p>

6190<p id="_1783" tabindex="-1">

6191For example, the American College of Medical Genetics and Genomics (ACMG) recommends that pathogenic variants in <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/clinvar/docs/acmg/" title="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/clinvar/docs/acmg/">

619273 genes</a>

6193, including 28 genes associated with 16 cancer/neoplastic syndromes should be reported any time an adult or child undergoes clinical genomic sequencing, regardless of the indication.[<a href="#cit/section_9.35">

619435</a>

6195-<a href="#cit/section_9.38">

619638</a>

6197] In 2019, the ACMG clarified its position, noting that the list of secondary findings genes was not intended or validated for use in population screening.[<a href="#cit/section_9.39">

619839</a>

6199] However, ACMG's working groups are exploring which genetic variants are the most important to disclose to asymptomatic individuals.[<a href="#cit/section_9.35">

620035</a>

6201]</p>

6202<p id="_1784" tabindex="-1">

6203The ACMG also recommends that individuals undergo an <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044677&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6204informed consent</a>

6205 process which allows them to opt out of receiving secondary findings.[<a href="#cit/section_9.40">

620640</a>

6207,<a href="#cit/section_9.41">

620841</a>

6209] Of note, various clinical programs, research programs, and laboratories have devised their own list of genes in which identified pathogenic variants could be released as secondary findings (for more information, see [<a href="#cit/section_9.42">

621042</a>

6211] [eMERGE] and [<a href="#cit/section_9.43">

621243</a>

6213] [MyCode/Geisinger]). In many cases, these gene lists are much broader than the one recommended by ACMG. ACMG's secondary findings list does not include several high-to-moderate cancer risk genes for which screening and risk-reduction measures may be recommended.[<a href="#cit/section_9.44">

621444</a>

6215,<a href="#cit/section_9.45">

621645</a>

6217] </p>

6218<p id="_1866" tabindex="-1">

6219However, for many of the genes on the ACMG list, the ACMG and others acknowledge the potential uncertainty about <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339344&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000339344&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6220penetrance</a>

6221 and, therefore, recommended medical management for individuals who test positive without relevant personal or (known) family history.[<a href="#cit/section_9.35">

622235</a>

6223,<a href="#cit/section_9.39">

622439</a>

6225] This consideration may add to the complexity of patient-provider decision making about expanding genetic testing to at-risk relatives.</p>

6226<p id="_1823" tabindex="-1">

6227In light of the complexities associated with possible outcomes of genomic sequencing, approaches to consenting patients about the types of results they would like to receive may include a discussion of the range of potential findings as opposed to a description of the medical implications for pathogenic variants in a host of specific genes.[<a href="#cit/section_9.46">

622846</a>

6229,<a href="#cit/section_9.47">

623047</a>

6231]</p>

6232<p id="_1824" tabindex="-1">

6233For example, pathogenic variants may be classified as medically actionable, such as those identified in <em class="gene-name">

6234BRCA1</em>

6235/<em class="gene-name">

6236BRCA2</em>

6237 or <em class="gene-name">

6238MSH2</em>

6239. Other variants may be clinically valid but are associated with a range of risk; these variants may have <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=803523&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000803523&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6240clinical utility</a>

6241 limited to specific circumstances, including the following:</p>

6242<div class="pdq-content-list">

6243<ul id="_1878">

6244<li>

6245Some genetic variants may be associated with modest increases in cancer risk.</li>

6246<li>

6247Pharmacogenomic variants generally do not predict disease risk but have clinical utility for individuals exposed to certain medications.</li>

6248<li>

6249Some variants may reveal an individual's carrier status for Mendelian conditions. These results may not be relevant for patients who do not have children or do not plan to have children. However, these results can have reproductive implications for relatives.</li>

6250<li>

6251Highly penetrant risk variants may be identified for which few options exist to lessen disease course or risk (e.g., for amyotrophic lateral sclerosis/Lou Gehrig&#8217;s disease or early onset Alzheimer disease).[<a href="#cit/section_9.47">

625247</a>

6253]</li>

6254</ul>

6255</div>

6256<p id="_1786" tabindex="-1">

6257Another consideration is that somatic testing of tumors may reveal pathogenic variants that, if confirmed in the germline, may have implications for both the patient tested (e.g., with respect to systemic treatment of the current cancer and risks for other cancers) and his or her relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_9.48">

625848</a>

6259-<a href="#cit/section_9.51">

626051</a>

6261] This concern may also arise in the context of immunohistochemistry (IHC) or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285933&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000285933&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6262microsatellite instability</a>

6263 (MSI) testing of colorectal cancer or uterine tumors, in which testing may be performed primarily to guide treatment of the patient, but subsequent germline testing may also determine whether the patient is affected with Lynch syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_9.52">

626452</a>

6265,<a href="#cit/section_9.53">

626653</a>

6267] Considerations about the implications for relatives and the potential benefits of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=799495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000799495&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6268cascade testing</a>

6269 in reducing morbidity and mortality from Lynch syndrome are particularly relevant given that universal testing of colorectal and uterine tumors is increasingly performed at the time of diagnosis, which may include tumor sequencing instead of IHC or MSI screening.[<a href="#cit/section_9.54">

627054</a>

6271,<a href="#cit/section_9.55">

627255</a>

6273] Thus, tests performed on tumor tissue, particularly when followed by confirmatory germline testing, may raise dual concerns about the <em>

6274duty to rescue</em>

6275 (the patient) and a possible <em>

6276duty to warn</em>

6277 at-risk relatives. One way to address these concerns is to have patients undergo an informed <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=689546&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000689546&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6278consent process</a>

6279 before any tumor testing to alert them about the importance and implications of germline testing for themselves and their relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_9.56">

628056</a>

6281-<a href="#cit/section_9.58">

628258</a>

6283] For more information, see the<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1742">

6284 Duty to warn considerations</a>

6285 section.</p>

6286</section>

6287</section>

6288<section id="_1787">

6289<h3 id="_1787_toc">

6290Employment and Insurance Discrimination</h3>

6291<p id="_1788" tabindex="-1">

6292Genetic information obtained from <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=256553&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000256553&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6293genetic susceptibility</a>

6294 tests may have medical, economic, and psychosocial implications for the individual tested and his or her family members. The potential for employment and insurance discrimination is a common concern for individuals considering genetic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_9.59">

629559</a>

6296-<a href="#cit/section_9.62">

629762</a>

6298] However, there is limited documentation of employment and insurance discrimination on the basis of hereditary cancer genetic testing results. For more information on discrimination associated with hereditary cancer genetic testing, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_226">

6299Informed Consent</a>

6300 section.</p>

6301<section id="_1790">

6302<h4 id="_1790_toc">

6303Legal proceedings, federal/state legislation, and recommendations of professional organizations regarding employment and insurance discrimination</h4>

6304<p id="_1791" tabindex="-1">

6305State and federal legislation statutes have been developed to prevent the use of genetic information for employment practices, such as hiring, promotion, and salary decisions; and insurance policies, including life and health coverage, by employers, schools, government agencies, and insurers.[<a href="#cit/section_9.63">

630663</a>

6307] According to <a href="https://www.transportation.gov/sites/dot.gov/files/docs/eo13145_0_0.pdf" title="https://www.transportation.gov/sites/dot.gov/files/docs/eo13145_0_0.pdf">

6308Executive Order 13145</a>

6309, federal departments and agencies are prohibited from discriminating against employees on the basis of genetic test results or information about a request for genetic testing services.[<a href="#cit/section_9.64">

631064</a>

6311] Employers and insurers are prohibited from intentionally lowering policy rates by using practices such as screening for individuals who are at risk of becoming ill or dying because of genetic disease susceptibility, such as cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_9.64">

631264</a>

6313] These provisions were extended by the Genetic Information Nondiscrimination Act (GINA) in 2008. For more information, see the <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1792">

6314GINA</a>

6315 section. Federal laws, including GINA, do not cover employer-provided life and disability insurance; however, some states do have legislation addressing the use of genetic information for life and disability policies. Current state statutes and bills may be found through <a href="https://www.genome.gov/about-genomics/policy-issues/Genome-Statute-Legislation-Database" title="https://www.genome.gov/about-genomics/policy-issues/Genome-Statute-Legislation-Database">

6316NHGRI's Genome Statute and Legislation Database</a>

6317, which is a useful resource for patients to consult before undergoing genetic testing. Examples of relevant legislation regarding genetic information are summarized in <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1100">

6318Table 2</a>

6319. The information in this table is not comprehensive but provides key points only. See the original sources for more information.</p>

6528<section id="_1792">

6529<h5 id="_1792_toc">

6530Genetic Information Nondiscrimination Act of 2008 (GINA)</h5>

6531<p id="_1793" tabindex="-1">

6532This U.S. federal law contains many protections against discrimination based on genetic information.[<a href="#cit/section_9.68">

653368</a>

6534-<a href="#cit/section_9.71">

653571</a>

6536] Examples of specific provisions are as follows:</p>

6537<div class="pdq-content-list">

6538<ul id="_1794">

6539<li>

6540Prohibits access to individuals&#8217; personal genetic information by insurance companies and by employers.</li>

6541<li>

6542Prohibits insurance companies from requesting that applicants for group or individual health coverage plans be subjected to genetic testing or screening and prohibits them from discriminating against health plan applicants on the basis of individual genetic information.</li>

6543<li>

6544Prohibits employers from using genetic information to refuse employment and prohibits them from collecting employees&#8217; personal genetic information without their explicit consent.</li>

6545<li>

6546Prohibits employment agencies from failing or refusing to refer a candidate on the basis of genetic information.[<a href="#cit/section_9.68">

654768</a>

6548]</li>

6549<li>

6550Prohibits labor organizations from refusing membership on the basis of a member's genetic make-up.[<a href="#cit/section_9.68">

655168</a>

6552]</li>

6553<li>

6554Does not mandate coverage for medical tests or treatments.[<a href="#cit/section_9.69">

655569</a>

6556]</li>

6557<li>

6558Does not prohibit medical underwriting based on current health status.[<a href="#cit/section_9.69">

655969</a>

6560]</li>

6561<li>

6562Does not limit a treating health provider, including those employed by or affiliated with health plans, from requesting or notifying individuals about genetic tests.[<a href="#cit/section_9.70">

656370</a>

6564]</li>

6565<li>

6566Does not prohibit occupational testing for toxic monitoring programs, employer-sponsored wellness programs, administration of federal and state family and medical leave laws, and certain cases of inadvertent acquisition of genetic information.[<a href="#cit/section_9.71">

656771</a>

6568]</li>

6569</ul>

6570</div>

6571<p id="_1795" tabindex="-1">

6572GINA amends and/or extends coverage of HIPAA, ADA, and the Employee Retirement Income Security Act by including genetic information under medical privacy and confidentiality legislation, and employment and insurance determinations. Additionally, with the passage of GINA, researchers and clinicians can encourage participation in clinical trials and appropriate genetic testing knowing that there are federal protections against discrimination on the basis of the results of genetic testing. GINA established the minimum protection level that must be met in all states. However, for states with more robust legislation in place, GINA does not weaken existing protections provided by state law.</p>

6573<p id="_1796" tabindex="-1">

6574However, GINA has several limitations, including the following:</p>

6575<div class="pdq-content-list">

6576<ol id="_1091">

6577<li>

6578GINA does not apply to members of the United States military, to veterans obtaining health care through the Veteran&#8217;s Administration, or to the Indian Health Service because the laws amended by GINA do not apply to these groups and programs.</li>

6579<li>

6580The legislation does not apply to life insurance, long-term care insurance, or disability insurance. Even though GINA does not provide protection for employer-provided disability and life insurance, some states do encompass these arenas in addition to employment, genetic privacy, health insurance, health insurance enforcement, life, disability, and long-term care. NHGRI's <a href="https://www.genome.gov/about-genomics/policy-issues/Genome-Statute-Legislation-Database" title="https://www.genome.gov/about-genomics/policy-issues/Genome-Statute-Legislation-Database">

6581Genome Statute and Legislation Database</a>

6582 provides a searchable listing of state statutes and bills related to the following topics: direct-to-consumer genetic testing, employment and insurance nondiscrimination, health insurance coverage, privacy, research, and the use of residual newborn screening specimens.</li>

6583<li>

6584GINA&#8217;s employment provisions generally do not apply to employers with fewer than 15 employees.[<a href="#cit/section_9.69">

658569</a>

6586]</li>

6587</ol>

6588</div>

6589<p id="_1797" tabindex="-1">

6590Under GINA, it is permissible for employers to request employees' genetic information for the purposes of voluntary wellness programs. However, employers cannot encourage employees to provide their genetic information; this means that if an employee chooses to give genetic information to the wellness program, they cannot receive an additional reward for doing so. Conversely, if an employee chooses to withhold genetic information, they cannot be penalized.[<a href="#cit/section_9.66">

659166</a>

6592] Regulations regarding workplace wellness programs have been amended by the U.S. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission, and they are in the process of further revision.[<a href="#cit/section_9.72">

659372</a>

6594] Thus, before providing genetic information to such wellness programs, patients should be informed about current regulations and provisions for privacy and confidentiality. </p>

6595</section>

6596<section id="_1798">

6597<h5 id="_1798_toc">

6598Exception to protections against employment and insurance discrimination: Military personnel</h5>

6599<p id="_1799" tabindex="-1">

6600GINA and other state and federal protections do not extend to genetic testing of active duty military personnel or genetic information obtained from active duty military personnel.[<a href="#cit/section_9.73">

660173</a>

6602] In the military, genetic testing provides medical information that is to be used to protect military personnel from potentially harmful duties or exposures that could stimulate or aggravate a health problem. For example, use of certain antimalaria medications in individuals with glucose 6-phosphate dehydrogenase deficiency can result in red blood cell rupture. Therefore, some genetic information may be critical for maintaining the health and safety of military personnel, given the possible stressful occupational environments they may face. In addition, all military personnel provide a <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45671&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000045671&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=genetic'); return(false);">

6603DNA</a>

6604 sample to be maintained in a repository that can be used for identification purposes.[<a href="#cit/section_9.74">

660574</a>

6606] </p>

6607<p id="_1800" tabindex="-1">

6608Results of genetic tests for disease predisposition could influence military eligibility for new enlistments. For current military personnel, genetic test results could influence worldwide eligibility, assignments, and promotions. </p>

6609<p id="_1801" tabindex="-1">

6610Thus, it is important for individuals who are considering enlisting in the military or those who are active duty to determine what specific policies apply to them, and what the implications of genetic testing may be for their current and future military career.[<a href="#cit/section_9.60">

661160</a>

6612] In addition, they should be aware of the potential implications of clinical and research genetic testing and possible concerns related to direct-to-consumer genomic testing.[<a href="#cit/section_9.75">

661375</a>

6614]</p>

6615</section>

6616</section>

6617</section>

6928 <section id="_1">

6929

6930 <h2>

6931Changes to This Summary (08/03/2023)</h2>

6932

6933 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

6934<p id="_3" tabindex="-1">

6935The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as new information becomes available. This section describes the latest changes made to this summary as of the date above.</p>

6936<p id="_1949" tabindex="-1">

6937<strong>

6938<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_5">

6939Introduction</a>

6940</strong>

6941</p>

6942<p id="_1946" tabindex="-1">

6943Revised <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_319">

6944text</a>

6945 to state that practice resources have been published to help clinicians identify individuals who may benefit from a genetics evaluation and/or genetic counseling. Organizations like the National Comprehensive Cancer Network (NCCN) frequently update related genetic counseling and genetic testing guidelines (cited NCCN [Genetic/Familial High-Risk Assessment: Breast, Ovarian, and Pancreatic Cancer] as reference 3, NCCN [Genetic/Familial High-Risk Assessment: Colorectal] as reference 4, and Bashford et al. as reference 6).</p>

6946<p id="_1947" tabindex="-1">

6947<strong>

6948<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1458">

6949Identification of Candidates for Referral to Genetic Counseling</a>

6950</strong>

6951</p>

6952<p id="_1948" tabindex="-1">

6953This section was extensively revised.</p>

6954<p id="_1950" tabindex="-1">

6955<strong>

6956<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_157">

6957Components of the Risk Assessment Process</a>

6958</strong>

6959</p>

6960<p id="_1951" tabindex="-1">

6961Added <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1555">

6962text</a>

6963 to state that American College of Medical Genetics (ACMG) and National Society of Genetic Counselors (NSGC) practice guidelines address tumor types, other potential features, and related criteria that would prompt a genetics referral. ACMG and NSGC state that the guidelines are intended to maximize appropriate referral of at-risk individuals for cancer genetics consultations, but they are not meant to provide genetic testing or treatment recommendations. However, they also note the importance of consulting other guidelines that are more frequently updated, like NCCN (cited Bashford et al. as reference 35).</p>

6964<p id="_1952" tabindex="-1">

6965<strong>

6966<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1608">

6967Risk Communication</a>

6968</strong>

6969</p>

6970<p id="_1953" tabindex="-1">

6971Added <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_128">

6972text</a>

6973 to state that a standard-of-care randomized controlled trial of 100 female <em class="gene-name">

6974BRCA1/BRCA2</em>

6975 carriers found positive psychological outcomes and improved sleep quality in those who participated in the 12-week Inquiry-Based Stress Reduction (IBSR) intervention. In this study, outcomes were measured 24 to 26 weeks after the IBSR intervention (cited Landau et al. as reference 21).</p>

6976<p id="_1954" tabindex="-1">

6977<strong>

6978<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1610">

6979Cancer Genetics Service Delivery</a>

6980</strong>

6981</p>

6982<p id="_1955" tabindex="-1">

6983The <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1927">

6984Targeted outreach and testing of high-risk populations</a>

6985 subsection was renamed from Screening of high-risk populations.</p>

6986<p id="_1960" tabindex="-1">

6987The <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1927">

6988Targeted outreach and testing of high-risk populations</a>

6989 subsection was extensively revised. </p>

6990<p id="_1956" tabindex="-1">

6991The <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1930">

6992Universal germline genetic testing approaches in oncology</a>

6993 was renamed from Universal genetic counseling and testing for affected patients.</p>

6994<p id="_1961" tabindex="-1">

6995The <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1930">

6996Universal germline genetic testing approaches in oncology</a>

6997 subsection was extensively revised.</p>

6998<p id="_1962" tabindex="-1">

6999Added <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1939">

7000Mainstreamed models in primary care</a>

7001 as a new subsection.</p>

7002<p id="_1963" tabindex="-1">

7003The <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1941">

7004Triaged services using nongenetics providers</a>

7005 subsection was renamed from Triaged or streamlined services using nongenetics providers.</p>

7006<p id="_1964" tabindex="-1">

7007The <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1941">

7008Triaged services using nongenetics providers</a>

7009 subsection was extensively revised.</p>

7010<p id="_1965" tabindex="-1">

7011<strong>

7012<a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_240">

7014</strong>

7016<p id="_1966" tabindex="-1">

7017Updated NCCN (Genetic/Familial High-Risk Assessment: Breast, Ovarian, and Pancreatic Cancer) as <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1784">

7018reference 44</a>

7019 and NCCN (Genetic/Familial High-Risk Assessment: Colorectal) as <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq#_1784">

7020reference 45</a>

7041 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

7042

7043 <h2>

7044About This PDQ Summary</h2>

7045

7046 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

7047<section id="_AboutThis_2">

7048<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

7049Purpose of This Summary</h3>

7050<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

7051This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about cancer genetics risk assessment and counseling. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

7052</section>

7053<section id="_AboutThis_4">

7054<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

7055Reviewers and Updates</h3>

7056<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

7057This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/genetics" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/genetics">

7058PDQ Cancer Genetics Editorial Board</a>

7059, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

7060<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

7061 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

7062<div class="pdq-content-list">

7063<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

7064<li>

7065be discussed at a meeting,</li>

7066<li>

7067be cited with text, or</li>

7068<li>

7069replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

7070</ul>

7071</div>

7072<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

7073Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

7074<p id="" tabindex="-1">

7075The lead reviewers for Cancer Genetics Risk Assessment and Counseling are:</p>

7076<div class="pdq-content-list">

7077<ul id="">

7078<li>

7079Kathleen A. Calzone, PhD, RN, AGN-BC, FAAN (National Cancer Institute)</li>

7080<li>

7081Suzanne C. O'Neill, PhD (Georgetown University)</li>

7082<li>

7083Susan K. Peterson, PhD, MPH (University of Texas, M.D. Anderson Cancer Center)</li>

7084<li>

7085John M. Quillin, PhD, MPH, MS (Virginia Commonwealth University)</li>

7086<li>

7087Charite Ricker, MS, CGC (University of Southern California)</li>

7088<li>

7089Catharine Wang, PhD, MSc (Boston University School of Public Health)</li>

7090</ul>

7091</div>

7092<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

7093Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

7094Email Us</a>

7095. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

7096</section>

7097<section id="_AboutThis_10">

7098<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

7099Levels of Evidence</h3>

7100<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

7101Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Cancer Genetics Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/genetics">

7102formal evidence ranking system</a>

7103 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

7104</section>

7105<section id="_AboutThis_12">

7106<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

7107Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

7108<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

7109PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

7110<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

7111The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

7112<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

7113PDQ&#174; Cancer Genetics Editorial Board. PDQ Cancer Genetics Risk Assessment and Counseling. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq">

7114https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq</a>

7115. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26389258]</p>

7116<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

7117Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

7118Visuals Online</a>

7119, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.
